% ---------- 简介（无页码） ----------
\vspace*{4cm}

\begin{center}
    \rule[0.5ex]{5cm}{0.8pt}\\[6pt]
    {\Large\scshape Introduction}\\[6pt]
    \rule[0.5ex]{5cm}{0.8pt}
\end{center}

\vspace{2cm}

{\centering
\color{gray!80!black}
\begin{minipage}{0.8\textwidth}
    \setstretch{1.6}
    \itshape
    \raggedright
    \setlength{\parindent}{1em}   
    Before summer vacation began, the Science Department Orienteering Competition was officially announced.

    A traditional event of Tsunanagai High School’s Science Department, it brought together selected elites from the Physics Club, Chemistry Club, and Biology Club, who would race across the city and compete against one another using scientific knowledge and wit.

    Among the formidable competitors, those chosen to represent the Biology Club were Izuta and Iwama.

    “This summer, let’s run our hardest together!”

    Yet Izuta harbored private concerns. Behind this event, meant to deepen bonds, an unexpected “incident” was quietly unfolding—one that could mar their youth.

    To protect his friends, and to protect Iwama. To ensure the Science Department Orienteering Competition could proceed safely, Izuta stood up alongside the friends who shared his secret.

    “As long as we’re together, we can surely build that one-of-a-kind logic.”
\end{minipage}
\par}

% ==============================
%        Prologue
% ==============================
\clearpage
\thispagestyle{plain}
\phantomsection
\begin{center}
    \large\bfseries
    Prologue\\
    I Love You So Much
\end{center}
\label{sec:prologueV3}
\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Prologue}
\vspace{1cm}

It was plainly a goodbye letter, but when I read it, all I thought was, ``I see. Just like I thought.'' I wasn't just bluffing.
I had been prepared for this for a long time.
This spring, both Iwama and I would finish our master's programs and go on to doctoral studies. It had been nearly six years since we entered university, and almost nine since we first met at Tsunanagai High School. That was more than enough time for us to see just how fundamentally different our outlooks were.

A polite goodbye from someone I had loved that deeply was, of course, hard to bear.

\textit{I believe we should not see each other anymore. I am sure you feel the same, Izuta-kun.}

But I could accept it.

It was because we knew each other so well that we had ended up here.

This was exactly like Iwama. Her neat handwriting, lined up perfectly across the page, was full of gratitude, and she had gone to great lengths to explain why there was no fixing what lay between us.

I was heading to Kyoto in the spring. Iwama was staying in Tokyo. We were both the type to throw ourselves into our research, so it was obvious we would drift apart whether we wanted to or not. It was the right time to end things.

This was Iwama's way of being kind, her way of thinking ahead for both our futures.

Even though we lived in the same world and devoted ourselves to the same field of biology, our eyes were fixed on completely different things.

While I was absorbed in tiny plants, Iwama had her eyes on society as a whole.
While I went into the unprofitable field of ecology, Iwama was working in cutting-edge medical research.
While I was pushing through the thickets on Iriomote Island, Iwama was giving a presentation at an academic conference in San Francisco.

If that had been all, maybe it would have been fine. But the fundamental gap in how we approached scholarship led to clash after clash. And because we both loved science with everything we had and had chosen to give our lives to it, neither of us could back down.

Looking back, the signs had been there ever since high school.

Back in those days, when everyone was oddly obsessed with outdoing each other, the difference in our talent had been impossible to ignore.

Iwama, who could shine as a generalist, and me, who could only survive as a specialist.

That unexpected summer had stripped bare the fundamental difference between us---

% ==============================
%        Chapter 1
% ==============================
\clearpage
\thispagestyle{plain}
\phantomsection
\begin{center}
    \large\bfseries
    Chapter 1\\
    An Unexpected Summer
\end{center}
\label{sec:ch1V3}
\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Chapter 1}
\vspace{1cm}

$\delta$

The regular exams at Tsunanagai High School were infamous for being so brutal they bordered on the absurd.

Maybe that was the result of adapting to this country's insane college entrance exam culture. No matter which of the five major subjects it was, they hit you with a ridiculous number of questions no one could possibly finish in time, plus ``Final Boss'' problems you still couldn't beat without luck even if you had studied properly.

``Basically, if you score a 75 here, that's about the same as a perfect score at a normal high school.''

Apparently, that was something the upperclassmen liked to say.

The passing line was about fifty percent. Get fifty points, and you got the credit. The average was set at around 60\%, and most students seemed to land somewhere between 50\% and 70\%.

If you scored 80\% total across all five subjects, people looked at you with awe and curiosity, and if you hit 90\%, they treated you like a monster.

Supposedly, someone monstrous enough to keep up a 90\% average only showed up once every two or three years.

Mizasaki gave me the quick rundown, then added one last detail with a look that said he was going for maximum dramatic effect.

``I heard Hongo-senpai has never scored below 95\%, not even once.''

``That doesn't surprise me much.''

\textit{No matter what new trait got tacked onto that venomous snake wearing a gyaru's skin, all I'd probably think was, I see. Just as I expected. Even if someone told me she'd started moving things with psychic powers, I'd probably just shrug and say, ``Yeah, sure. That sounds about right.''}

I fanned the collar of my summer uniform with a clipboard as I pulled out my cherry tomato bento. Beyond the window stretched a deep blue sky and towering cumulus clouds. The only reason we were talking about exams in the middle of such a perfect lunch break was because finals had ended just last week, and this was the time of year when graded test papers started getting handed back in every class.

It was already July, and as long as you didn't fail anything, summer vacation was just around the corner. As you'd expect from the top prep school in the prefecture, it wasn't just Mizasaki---our other classmates were buzzing about their scores too.

Apparently, that even applied to the person I'd least expected to get worked up over something like this.

``Del-chan! This is amazing! You made it on!''

Iwama, who had returned to the classroom, invited me into the hallway with sparkling eyes. She looked seconds away from grabbing me by the wrist, so I set down my bento and stood up on my own. Mizasaki trailed after us too, curious about what was going on.

Led by Iwama, we headed to the second-floor hall. A crowd of first-years had gathered there, all visibly excited. I could hear their phone cameras clicking as they snapped photos.

High on the wall hung a B4-sized poster. It had a simple table printed on it.

It was the ranking for the finals and year-end exams---the notorious ``Eight Immortals List.''

\fullpageimage{1.jpg}

\thispagestyle{plain}
\vspace{1cm}

As if to say \textit{you'll know it when you see it}, it had no title---just rank, name, class, and score laid out in plain view. What made it nasty was that it even included the score breakdown for each subject.

In other words, the top eight scorers on the recent finals were being publicly displayed.

I had already expected to see my name there. I had heard my entrance exam scores were the highest ``among the in-prefecture group'' and ``in the science stream,'' so making the top eight was only to be expected.

Still, I was surprised. Other than three of them, every face in the top eight was one I knew well.

``Whoa, Class C is amazing! Delta, Gamma-san, and even Mikage-san are on here!''

Standing beside me, Mizasaki blurted it out without a shred of shame. \textit{Since I knew how the class placements had really been decided, I knew this concentration of names wasn't a coincidence.}

Iwama, perhaps embarrassed by that,

``I see, so they average out the scores for each subject,''

said, keeping her comment safely neutral. Mizasaki was simple enough to take the bait and immediately changed the subject.

``Looks like they just drop the decimals. Delta, you got a 93 and a 100 in science, right? Took a bit of a hit there.''

I didn't really feel like I'd gotten the short end of the stick, but Mizasaki was right, so I just gave a silent shrug.

Even ``science'' wasn't just one subject---it was split into fields like physics, chemistry, and biology. This time, for example, science had been made up of ``Basic Chemistry'' and ``Basic Biology''---though calling those exams ``basic'' was a bad joke---and I had scored 93 and 100 respectively. So even though the average should have been 96.5, they dropped the decimal and left me with 96.

``Looks like even after we split into humanities and sciences in our second year and start taking different electives, they'll still keep up this five-subject ranking for the whole grade by adjusting the scores with averages. They really thought this through!''

``Well, rankings are fun, aren't they? It feels good when the person you're rooting for makes it to the top.''

``Don't talk about people like we're racehorses.''

``I'm not betting money, so don't worry.''

With Iwama standing right there, all I could do was sigh at Mizasaki when he threw me a wink.

Back in middle school, Mizasaki used to hang around with Osawa and Todo---the physics club guys---and place bets on the score battles between me and Watari---that obnoxious physics club bastard who never did anything but talk trash about me---as we fought over the top spot in our grade. Apparently, they used to bet things like slightly overpriced snacks. Getting riled up like that lit a fire under us, and the result of that all-out academic arms race was the performance I had now.

But as I looked at the ranking, one thought crossed my mind.

\textit{In the end, the middle school world where Watari and I had competed was nothing but a tiny little pond.}

I could have guessed that the elite ``out-of-prefecture group'' recruited from outside and the ``exception group'' who outdid them would be at the top, but seeing Mikage slip right in between the two of us---known as the ``Twin Pillars,'' always standing side by side like actual twins---still caught me off guard.

``Just like you, Delta. That score's insane.''

A voice suddenly whispered that close to me that this time, I really did feel like I'd been blindsided.

\fullpageimage{2.jpg}

\thispagestyle{plain}
\vspace{1cm}

Kannabi, who had come over still carrying her things from fourth period, was standing right behind me for some reason. Her voice was strikingly pleasant up close, but having it whispered straight into my ear was more than I could handle.

``I'd lose to a certain someone.''

Looking again at the name in second place, I accidentally let my true feelings slip.

Hijiri Mitsuyuki. We had been companions on a little grand adventure during Golden Week two months earlier. I'd heard he had gone to the same middle school as Kannabi. I had known he was extraordinary, but I'd never imagined there'd be a gap of nearly fifty points on a 500-point exam. Even though he was in the humanities stream, a closer look showed he had scored an 80 in math---higher than me. \textit{Apparently, it wasn't just his memory that was extraordinary.}

``The top three really are on a different level, aren't they?''

``They're incredible. On an exam that hard, they all scored over eighty percent. First place even broke ninety.''

Mizasaki, quick to jump on it, picked up on Kannabi's comment from across the room. Iwama was fired up too.

``Being surrounded by so many amazing people really makes you feel like you have to give it everything you've got!''

She was practically burning with competitive spirit.

Since there was no point standing around forever staring at the rankings in the middle of the crowd, we headed back to the classroom and resumed lunch. I could finally get back to my sweet cherry tomatoes.

``Why is that ranking list only eight people?''

While eating a small rice ball in her little hands, Kannabi raised a perfectly reasonable question.

``The `Eight Immortals List,' huh. No idea if it's true, but I've heard the rumor. Apparently, anyone whose name shows up on it even once gets into their first-choice university without exception. In other words, if you look at the line of students at this school who have never failed to get into their first choice, it comes out to the top eight.''

\textit{Did that mean there had once been a student who ranked ninth and failed to get into their first-choice school?}

At Mizasaki's dubious rumor of unknown origin, Iwama stopped her chopsticks and looked at me.

``That... sounds like a lot to carry.''

``Maybe I'll skip putting MIT or Harvard as my first choice.''

I meant it as a joke, but Iwama's eyes went wide.

``Del-chan, are you thinking about going overseas?''

``No way. At the very least, I'm staying in Japan for undergrad. I don't want to take classes in English.''

``That's good.''

\textit{I didn't know what exactly was so ``good'' about that, but somehow, I felt like Iwama and I would probably end up at the same university.}

\textit{And for some reason, that felt ``good'' to me too.}

$\gamma$

The sports festival for the science-track students.

A brutal contest of total ability, where you fought with your body by using your brain.

An all-out battle of intellect where tactics, betrayal, deception, and even exploiting loopholes in the rules were all fair game.

I'd been hearing rumors about `Science Club Ori' for a while now. They came from all sorts of people, one after another, and always with a hint of excitement. \textit{It had to be the kind of event that drew that much attention.}

It was almost certain that I would be chosen as one of the representatives alongside Del-chan. \textit{Apparently, the usual practice was to pick the top scorers across the five subjects as the representatives.}

\textit{It wasn't as though I had worked hard just for this. But I was glad I had prepared for the exams properly.}

\textit{This summer, I would make my way through it alongside the person I loved.}

The day the `Eight Immortals List' went up also happened to be the day of the Science Club Ori briefing. After school, the four of us first-year biology club members stayed together and took seats toward the back of the audiovisual room. The room was just barely large enough to hold all 240 students in our year, and it was packed with members of the Tsunanagai High School Science Club.

When I say ``Science Club,'' I don't mean some university faculty. It was the umbrella name for the science-related clubs: the Physics Club, the Chemistry Club, and the Biology Club. Apparently, there had also once been a Mathematics Club and an Earth Science Club, but as their numbers dwindled, they were folded into the Physics Club. \textit{Incidentally, the Biology Club had been on the verge of being shut down, but our joining had saved it.}

The Biology Club consisted of the four of us plus our third-year, Karato-senpai, for a total of five members. By contrast, the Physics and Chemistry clubs each seemed to have around thirty to forty members at a glance. There were about eighty people in the entire Science Club. Since it was still before summer vacation and the third-years hadn't really retired yet, that worked out to about twenty-seven people per year.

\textit{In other words, one out of every ten students at this high school belonged to the Science Club. That was an incredible ratio.}

Then, all at once, the noisy audiovisual room fell silent.

I saw the three club presidents climb onto the stage.

``Alright. Thanks for settling down. We'll now begin the briefing for `Science Club Ori.'''

The one holding the microphone was Mikage Akifumi-senpai, the president of the Physics Club. He looked athletic, with broad shoulders and short hair styled with wax. In fact, he was supposedly genuinely good at sports too. And by ``too,'' I meant that Mikage-senpai's real defining trait was his intellect. Despite being a local, he belonged to the `Exceptional Group' whose grades surpassed even the `Out-of-prefecture Group' specially recruited from outside the prefecture. While holding on to second place in the year, he also served as Student Council President and had reportedly solved one problem after another with brilliant skill.

And the one who had always kept Mikage-senpai stuck in second was the person standing beside him, Hongo Nagisa-senpai. She was the Chemistry Club president. She had flashy blonde hair and flawless makeup. She was part of the `Out-of-prefecture Group' recruited from Tokyo and had apparently held the number-one spot in the year ever since enrollment. She also served as Student Council Vice President.

Incidentally, those two were dating. They were the ``best couple'' in the most literal sense possible.

Watching over those two, who were visibly close, was our own Karato Rora-senpai. She was the president of the Biology Club, with unruly hair and black-rimmed glasses as her defining features. Her family ran a veterinary hospital, and we learned everything we knew about caring for living things from Karato-senpai.

Hongo-senpai used a remote to dim the lights, then worked the PC set on the lectern at the side of the stage. A pure white image appeared on the large screen at the front.

``Alright, everyone, good job on your finals. I'm Mikage, president of the Physics Club. I'm sure you've already heard about this in your respective clubs, but I'd like to officially announce it here one more time.''

When Mikage-senpai glanced her way, Hongo-senpai pressed a button on the remote.

Large, bold black characters appeared on the white screen.

\begin{center}
\textbf{2024 Science Club Ori --- Officially Announced!}
\end{center}

``This summer too, the Science Club will be holding its grand annual tradition, `Science Club Ori'!''

Applause broke out at once. I joined in with an enthusiastic round of applause myself.

``Nice applause. Alright then, let's jump straight into the overview. Since all of you just finished exams and are used to reading, I figure it'll be quicker for you to look at a PowerPoint than listen to me explain everything.''

The screen changed to a slide filled with concise bullet points.

\begin{center}
\textbf{What is Science Club Ori?}
\end{center}

\circled{1} It is both a ``Science Club Orientation'' and a ``Science Club Orienteering''

\circled{2} The main goal is to strengthen the bonds between the Physics, Chemistry, and Biology clubs!

\circled{3} Selected freshmen will take on a science-style orienteering competition that tests both brains and physical ability.

``Alright, finished reading? In a nutshell, it's an event where we all get closer by playing and running a fun science-based orienteering game together. By the way, is there anyone here who doesn't really know what orienteering is?''

When Mikage-senpai raised his hand, a few others went up here and there in response. Beside me, Rei timidly raised her hand too. \textit{It seemed Del-chan and Mizasaki-kun already knew what it was.}

``Right, figured as much. I'll explain. First, the word `orient' comes from a Latin word meaning the direction where the sun rises---in other words, the East. I guess the direction of sunrise mattered a lot in the old days. From there, it came to mean `to position' or `to face a certain direction.'''

Even though it had suddenly turned into an English lesson, nobody laughed.

``That's why an `orientation' is an event that shows newcomers what direction an organization is headed in---basically, `this is what kind of place this is.' And the more important one here, `orienteering,' basically means a competition in `finding your way.'''

The slide changed. A website that looked like it belonged to an orienteering organization appeared on the screen. It showed a photo of a man running along a mountain trail with a strained look on his face.

``Originally, it's a grueling competition where you use nothing but a map and compass to find several checkpoints in order, then compete over how fast you can pass through them and make it to the goal. But of course---''

Mikage-senpai paused for a moment there and grinned.

``There's no point asking science clubs to rely on physical strength alone. That's why Science Club Ori has special rules designed to make a scientific mind more important than pure running speed. I'll go over the details on the day, but it's a three-way team battle held over two days. It'll take place right at the start of summer vacation---next weekend, to be exact. You all kept your schedules open, right?''

``Yeah!'' some of the boys answered cheerfully. The senior gave them a casual one-handed wave of thanks and continued.

``Now then, while the freshmen are the ones who participate in the orienteering every year, only a small number of selected members actually get to play, since it requires a high level of scientific knowledge. Still, I hope the rest of you won't be too disappointed. The joy of working behind the scenes---with no irony whatsoever---is waiting for you.''

The slide advanced, displaying an overview of the event.

\begin{center}
\textbf{Overview of Science Club Ori}
\end{center}

\begin{itemize}
\item Held over two days, July 20 (Sat) to July 21 (Sun)
\item The setting is the entire Tsunanagai city area
\item We will be staying overnight at the school with special permission from the principal
\item This year's orienteering will be a three-way team battle
\item Two selected members will be chosen from the freshmen of each club
\item Everyone else will handle preparation, event management, and watching the action
\end{itemize}

After giving everyone a little time to read, Mikage-senpai spoke in an amused tone.

``I think you can tell just by looking at this, but the selected members are basically the guest roles. Whether it's a school festival or a death game, the people running things are the ones having the most fun, right? The real essence of Science Club Ori is making the guests into sacrifices while the rest of us set up the game, watch it unfold, and deepen our friendships together.''

\textit{Since I'd never been part of a death game, I couldn't really picture it, but seeing the upperclassmen---who had probably already been through `Science Club Ori'---nodding along, I figured it really must be like that. Or rather, was the science club's orienteering really brutal enough to be compared to a death game?}

``...Now that the general explanation is over, I'm sure you're all waiting for the announcement of the sacrifi---I mean, the selected members. This year, we're happy to have the Biology Club back, so we'll be selecting two freshmen from each club! Those whose names are called, please come forward.''

\textit{My heart started pounding like an alarm bell. No one had told me anything about having to come forward.} Keeping my parasympathetic nervous system in mind, I took deep breaths to force my heart rate back down. \textit{Acetylcholine, acetylcholine.}

``First, from our own Physics Club. Our first pick. Surprisingly, for the first time in years, we've selected a freshman who plans to go into the humanities stream. Hijiri Mitsuyuki-kun!''

Applause broke out. A boy with his hair slicked back stood up from the Physics Club section. With perfect posture and no particular expression on his face, he walked up onto the stage. His rimless glasses glinted.

He was from the same Sunamizu Middle School as Rei, and apparently he and Del-chan were close enough to have bathed together. He was a prodigy who had taken second place in the year with an overwhelming score on these finals.

``And our second pick. I heard he was in the physics club in middle school too. Watari Marejou-kun!''

More applause. From the seat right next to where Hijiri-kun had been sitting, a tough-looking boy with somewhat long brown-dyed hair stood up. He was from the same middle school as Del-chan and Mizasaki-kun, and apparently he was very close to both of them.

While Watari-kun was heading up to the stage, the microphone was passed to Hongo-senpai.

``Now, now, let's move on to the Chemistry Club members! Our first pick is... a huge surprise! A brand-new member who joined during exam season! Togakushi Shoma-kun!''

At that, even the Chemistry Club members gasped in surprise. For a moment, I thought no one was going to respond---but when I looked around, I saw a boy stand up in the very back, even behind us, in the seat by the entrance.

As he passed right by me on his way to the stage, I felt something about his presence that made my spine tingle.

\textit{It might have been rude to think that about a fellow student. But he felt inhuman.}

Pure white hair. A hunched back, and a languid way of walking with his hands in his pockets. And yet, an extraordinary sense of intimidation radiated from his entire body. The eyes I glimpsed behind his bangs were red and bloodshot.

That was when I remembered that the name Togakushi had been sitting at the very top of the `Eight Immortals List.'

Hongo-senpai greeted him with a smile and gave his shoulder a familiar pat before continuing the announcements.

``Second pick! It's been all boys so far, but we've got a girl too! Is this the first time this surname's been called here in two years? Mikage Aya-san!''

The familiar figure of Mikage-san stood up. The Chemistry Club members, shaken by the unexpected first announcement, gave her a big round of applause.

``Um, okay, lastly, I'll announce the Biology Club members.''

Karato-senpai, having received the microphone from Hongo-senpai, spoke in an unconfident voice.

``Our first pick is, let's see, how do you read this... Sho? Yes. Izuta Sho-kun!''

Prompted in a whisper by Mikage-senpai, the senior called out Del-chan's name with the air of someone blurting out a spoiler.

Amid the applause, Del-chan got to his feet after Mizasaki-kun patted him on the back, looking anything but pleased. Meanwhile, Karato-senpai impatiently spoke up again.

``And our second pick is Gamma---I mean, Iwama-san. Iwama Rio-saa-an!''

With my name called in the same tone as someone being summoned for a checkup at a hospital, I stood up a little amused. Since Del-chan was still right beside me, we ended up heading down the aisle together.

Del-chan glanced back at me for a moment, and his expression alone seemed to say, \textit{Looks like the trouble's only going to keep piling up.} I answered by clenching one hand in front of my chest as if to say, \textit{Let's do our best together!}

Walking together through the narrow aisle of the audiovisual room to the sound of applause felt just like walking down a runway.

\textit{An event I knew nothing about. I felt a faint sense of foreboding too. But this might have been the first time in my life I had ever looked forward to a competition this much.}

$\delta$

``Man, I had no idea. To think the top student in the year joined the Chemistry Club.''

After the briefing ended, the five of us from Class 1-C decided to head home together, and after seeing Iwama and Kannabi off at the station. As the three of us remaining walked through the shopping district in front of the station, Mizasaki happily babbled on like that.

``It was sudden. Though I had overheard that that woman had been aggressively trying to recruit him ever since the welcome party.''

Mikage, as usual, referred to Hongo-senpai---his brother's girlfriend---as ``that woman.'' \textit{I thought he should just be honest and congratulate his precious brother on his happiness, but well, I suppose there were circumstances.}

``Aren't the seniors putting a little too much effort into recruiting? Every selected member this time was someone from that `Eight Immortals List,' right?''

``They probably picked them based on their scores across the five subjects, so I don't think it's especially strange.''

``But six out of eight? And judging by their scores, the other two are pure humanities types. If anything, they even pulled in the top student on the humanities side, so to me it looks like they're scooping up all the top achievers.''

\textit{He really was carefree, talking like this was casual small talk at some draft meeting.}

``If you gather talented people, the club naturally gets livelier. I'm sure the upperclassmen were aware of that. My br---I want to eat meat.''

I thought she would forcibly correct her slip of the tongue like usual, but Mikage gave up on even that and started saying something nonsensical instead. \textit{It certainly was about time to be getting hungry, but it was far too sudden.}

``I want meat too. I'm seriously jealous. Mikage-san, do you think you can win?''

It sounded like Mizasaki had started saying something off-topic too, but this actually made some sense. It was said that the winners of `Science Club Ori' would be granted ``the greatest honor you can brag about forever'' and ``a dinner at a top-class yakiniku restaurant.'' Apparently, you could stuff yourself with meat so expensive your eyes would pop out.

``Hmm, who knows. Everyone's talented, and all I can do is math.''

``There you go again.''

``I mean, the Chemistry Club even has the year's number one. That guy looks strong just from looking at him. He's got white hair, after all.''

``Don't measure strength by hair color. Has anyone even shown there's a correlation?''

``Come on, aren't a huge percentage of the strongest characters white-haired?''

``What world are those statistics from?''

After we slipped into our usual nonsense conversation out of sheer habit, Mikage, who had been listening, started to giggle.

``I wonder what kind of person Togakushi-kun is. I've heard he's from northern Hokkaido. Apparently, he only comes to class the absolute minimum number of days, like he's walking a tightrope, and hardly comes to school at all.''

``What's with that? What a terrible guy! Is he lazing around in some mansion while living off a special allowance?''

``He's still getting results grade-wise, so you can't really complain.''

``True enough. Exactly ninety percent---450 points, was it? When someone puts up numbers like that, there's not much you can say.''

Mizasaki was the kind of guy who immediately wanted to talk about scores. Mikage played along.

``It was an interesting score spread. No perfect marks, but he was nearly flawless in everything except Japanese.''

``He got a 63 in Japanese. Even I only barely beat him there.''

``I wonder what kind of study method makes you lose that many points in Japanese when everything else is so high,'' I said without much thought, but Mikage took it seriously.

``There was one other thing I found strange. Togakushi-kun got a 99 in math, right?''

``That's right. You were probably the only one with a perfect score, Mikage-san.''

``I can understand Watari-kun's 96. If you missed the hardest part of the final problem, you'd probably lose points like that. But for someone who got everything else perfectly right to have only one point taken off... I wonder what happened there.''

Even after hearing all that, I still didn't understand what Mikage found so strange.

``Well, why not just ask him yourself next time?''

``...You're right. Sorry. I was getting hung up on something weird.''

``It's fine. I guess there's just a world up in the clouds that only people like you can understand...''

The days had gotten quite long, and it was still bright on the way home. Mikage lived in the western part of the city, while Mizasaki and I lived in the east. We reached the intersection where we would part ways, but the conversation kept going, as if neither of us really wanted to leave yet.

``Anyway, I'm looking forward to it. A brutal orienteering event for science kids, competing in both brains and physical ability. An all-out intellectual mixed martial arts match. And on top of that, the details stay secret until the actual day. Do your best and make it entertaining. I'm rooting for Delta and Mikage-san about equally.''

``If anything, root for me a little more,'' I joked, but I was actually pretty anxious. \textit{Competing itself was fine, but being one of the few selected members felt like a heavy responsibility. On top of that, the rest of the club would be watching us. It was a team battle, but our opponents included high-achievers on a completely different level, like Hijiri and Togakushi. Mikage and Watari had extraordinary mathematical instincts. If athletic ability came into play too, I was no match for Hijiri or Watari.}

``Of course, my number-one favorite is the Delta-Gamma pair. But I want Mikage-san to do well too, okay?''

``Don't worry about it. I'm rooting hard for Delta-kun and Gamma-san too.''

\textit{The loaded way she said it bothered me, but pointing it out now would probably just dig my grave deeper. There was no way I could bring myself to say it sounded like she was cheering on a romance.}

``Ah, Mikage-san too? I really think they'd make a great match.''

``Yeah. It almost makes me a little jealous.''

Since I stayed quiet, the two of them just kept saying whatever they pleased.

\textit{Whatever I felt myself, it was still just plain rude to make assumptions about Iwama's feelings like that.}

``See you tomorrow.''

Just then, the light turned green, so I grabbed Mizasaki---who still looked like he wanted to keep talking---and started across the street. Mikage waved cheerfully at us from the sidewalk.

$\gamma$

I passed through the ticket gate with Rei and waited for the train on the evening platform. From Tsunanagai Station by the coast, you could see a bit of the ocean, and the wind carried a faint scent of the tide. Since we were taking the inbound train, I was glad it wasn't very crowded despite it being the evening rush hour.

``Being a selected member sounds like a lot of work.''

Rei, who had held back while the five of us were talking, offered me another few words of concern.

I nodded and steered the conversation in a more positive direction.

``But I'm looking forward to it too. I almost feel a little bad getting to enjoy myself so much with something everyone else worked so hard to prepare.''

``It seems like they're doing it on a very large scale.''

``Right? I was surprised they even have corporate sponsors. It's the real thing!''

Apparently, Science Club Ori involved setting up all sorts of gimmicks throughout the Tsunanagai city area. To do that, they had to get permission from landowners, secure funding from corporate sponsors, and receive supplies high schoolers normally couldn't get on their own---they were putting in far more preparation than I had imagined.

``I'm sure people from the sponsoring companies will come watch too. If it were me, that alone would make me nervous.''

``I'll have to do my best so I don't let them down.''

The train pulled into the platform with a metallic screech. Our conversation was cut off for a moment as the noise swallowed our voices. The train slowly came to a stop, the doors opened with a hiss, and we boarded.

There were a few empty seats here and there, but since there weren't two together, we stayed standing side by side. Rei pointed to an empty seat in front of us, but I shook my head and turned it down.

\textit{If I ended up sitting alone, I'd just stand anyway, and besides, it was easier to talk when we were side by side.}

``But still, since it's you, Rio, I guess there's nothing to worry about.''

Rei picked the conversation right back up as if there had been no interruption at all.

``I'm not that amazing. But you're right---since it's a team battle, Del-chan will be with me too.''

``I do kind of get the impression that Delta's not very strong when it comes to competition, though...''

``I feel more at ease when I'm with Del-chan.''

Rei turned to look at me. Under the warmth of her gaze, I realized how my own words must have sounded. \textit{I flustered in spite of myself, and my ears grew hot. It was probably the adrenaline.}

``Hey, Rio, are you---''

Rei started to say something, then abruptly shut her mouth halfway through. The train was beginning to decelerate. It would soon arrive at Sunamizu Station, where Rei would get off. \textit{I wished I could just assume she stopped because the conversation was about to be interrupted anyway---but unfortunately, my brain wasn't built that conveniently.}

\textit{I could guess what Rei was thinking. It wasn't exactly a case of though I try to hide it, but every now and then, I became unbelievably bad at pretending to be innocent.}

Before getting off the train, Rei gave me a bit of advice.

``There's still a little time, so why not try studying together before Science Club Ori?''

I put on a face as though I'd taken it as advice for the competition rather than for romance and replied, ``That's a good idea!''

Until I reached Ebiwakagawa, I quietly sorted through my thoughts while gazing at the evening scenery sliding past the train window.

\textit{My high school life had taken an unexpected turn.}

\textit{Even though I had never thought something like this would happen, I had probably fallen in love.}

\textit{With the person who found the sakura inome with me on the mountain behind the school that beautiful spring.}

\textit{Of course, this could just have been me forcing my feelings into a shape I'd seen so often in novels and the like, and it wasn't necessarily certain that it was romantic.}

\textit{In a way, my feelings for Del-chan were similar to how I felt about science.}

\textit{I want to know more. I want to be more involved. Once I start thinking, I can't stop.}

\textit{This must be what it means to ``like'' someone.}

\textit{And it was a special kind of ``like'' at that.}

\textit{I was no longer at a stage where I could deny it.}

\textit{It wasn't as if I couldn't make excuses like ``I just have a strong interest'' or ``it's natural to be curious about things I don't know,'' but those were nothing more than word games.}

\textit{What kind of competition will `Science Club Ori' be?}

\fullpageimage{3.jpg}

\thispagestyle{plain}
\vspace{1cm}

\textit{I have no intention of brushing off my own emotions as mere wordplay.}

\textit{Whether this is romantic love or not, I intend to properly acknowledge that I like Del-chan.}

\textit{The problem is exactly where that emotion came from.}

\textit{Every emotion must have a reason. At the very least, that is the rule I impose on myself. I can almost understand how someone who accidentally bumps into a utility pole might lash out at it and shout, ``That hurts, you bastard!''---but I do not want to be like that.}

\textit{Emotions should only arise when there is a legitimate reason for them.}

\textit{Therefore, emotions without a legitimate reason must be suppressed. Even if they cannot be suppressed, they must never be allowed to show on the surface.}

\textit{Normal human relationships are governed by reason. In short, that reason is the ability to share the logic behind one's actions and expressions. If someone close to you repeatedly behaves in incomprehensible ways or shows irrational emotions, the relationship becomes a source of stress. That is why people who lash out at utility poles are probably bad at maintaining normal relationships; it is difficult to share logic with them.}

\textit{I need to think seriously about this. Given that it is a fact that I feel a special fondness for Del-chan, and that it might leak out as it did earlier, is the logic behind it something the people around me could understand?}

\textit{If there is no logic behind my affection, then what is the fundamental difference between me showing fondness for Del-chan and some careless person lashing out at a utility pole they walked into?}

\textit{The truth is, I still do not understand the logic behind it.}

\textit{I do not really know why I have fallen in love with Del-chan myself.}

\textit{Of course, since we are a boy and a girl, I could blame it on animal impulses that cannot be explained by reason. But that is not me. I do not feel in the slightest that just any boy would do, and I do not think I could fall for a complete stranger even if he had the same appearance and abilities as Del-chan.}

\textit{So, why?}

\textit{Even now, I am still searching for that reason.}

$\delta$

I was not very---or rather, not at all---used to spending my days off with a girl other than my younger sister.

Perhaps because I was so focused on talking with Iwama as we walked side by side, I clipped my shoulder against a utility pole.

``Ow,'' I muttered reflexively.

``Oh no, are you okay? I'm sorry, it's because I didn't move over.''

I rubbed my shoulder and gave the flustered Iwama a wry smile.

``It's not your fault. It's the utility pole's fault for not watching where it was going.''

If I blamed myself, kindhearted Iwama would only worry more. I figured I could settle the matter by shifting the blame onto a third party---even if it was an object---but for some reason, that struck Iwama as hilarious. All I could do was watch as she laughed so hard it seemed to hurt for a while.

It was a Saturday afternoon under the midsummer sun. The two of us were on our way to the Municipal Central Library. Surprisingly, this was not some scheme of Mizasaki's or one of Kannabi's meddling moves. Iwama had invited me.

`If you're free tomorrow, why don't we study together?'

On Friday, after the Science Club Ori briefing, I checked my phone when I got home and found that invitation message from Iwama. I had found it odd to be studying at a time when even the freshmen at a prep school were thinking of nothing but summer vacation now that finals were over, but after thinking it over a little, I understood the purpose.

It was preparation for Science Club Ori.

Iwama and I, as the selected members representing the Biology Club, were set to take part in the grueling science-themed orienteering that required both brains and physical ability. The event was next weekend, so there was hardly any time left; this was probably an attempt to cram in whatever knowledge we could at the last minute.

Since I thought we were only going to study, I had come in casual clothes---a simple short-sleeved shirt and light long pants---but Iwama was dressed stylishly enough that I wondered whether she had plans to go on a date with someone afterward. Except that she was carrying her usual school bag, stuffed to the brim with books and other things.

``How long are you planning to stay today?''

When I asked, Iwama did not seem to have any particular plans, and she thought for a moment.

``Hmm, let's see. I'm fine staying until closing, but what about you, Del-chan?''

``I don't have any special plans tonight. It sounds like my parents and sister are eating out somewhere. Any time works for me.''

``Then let's keep at it until we're satisfied!''

\textit{Being able to look that happy over going to study was a talent in itself.}

Before long, the Municipal Central Library came into view. The curved building, stretching from the basement to the third floor, had a modern feel that gave it a playful, cheerful impression. It had more than just a bookstore inside---it even had a cafe, a burger shop, and a kids' space---so calling it an amusement complex might have been more fitting. Since it was Saturday, there were plenty of families around, along with quite a few foreigners who clearly were not locals. It seemed to have become something of a tourist spot as well.

The library also had plenty of spaces for self-study. From booth-style areas for quiet study to a lounge where you could talk while browsing books, and even an open space where food and drinks were allowed. Apparently, local residents could even rent meeting rooms. We checked the lounge area first, and when we saw there were some open seats, we headed straight for the academic books section. Conveniently, the shelves lined with illustrated guides and science books were right next to the lounge. It was probably a convenient setup for moving back and forth between the table and the shelves.

``Right now, I'm worried about physics. It's probably too late for math anyway, so I want to focus on physics.''

``Me too! We both want to shore up our weak points. Even for math, it probably wouldn't hurt to know general trivia-type things. Like how to solve the Tower of Hanoi.''

``I see. So it's not guaranteed they'll just have us solve math problems straight up.''

``If the point is to have people watch us, I think they'll use problems that are interesting to watch other people solve.''

\textit{I was impressed. I had only been thinking about the part where we needed a scientific mind, but if I considered the perspective and intentions of the people making the questions, I could somewhat predict what kind of tasks might come up.}

Science Club Ori was neither just an exam nor just a race. According to Mikage-senpai, we selected members would become players in a grueling game while the other club members watched. He had even said that watching was the fun part. It was not hard to imagine that they would put together a game worth watching.

The two of us picked out some interesting-looking books from the physics and math shelves and brought them back to the lounge area. Figuring side-by-side seats would make it easier to read the same books together, we took two seats at the end of a large square table.

``But there's probably no sport less suited to spectating than orienteering.''

When I said that, Iwama nodded.

``There'll probably be a lot of moving around, and I'm sure they'll make it so the course isn't known in advance.''

``I can't imagine they'd have guests from the sponsoring companies standing around at checkpoints under the blazing sun, either.''

``About that...''

There was no one nearby, but Iwama lowered her voice anyway.

``There's a rumor they might broadcast us this year.''

``Broadcast? Like television?''

``Rei told me. Apparently, the Physics Club originally made a point of putting effort into filming experiments, but lately they've started bringing impressive equipment like drones and LiveUs into the physics prep room.''

``What's a LiveU?''

\textit{It was probably different from ``I love you.'' It was a word I had never heard before.}

Iwama wrote ``LiveU'' in the corner of her notebook.

``It's a system for transmitting and receiving video using multiple mobile data lines. There's a lot of delay, and it can't be used in areas without signal, but just by connecting a backpack-sized device to a camera, you can send high-quality video in real time. I heard television stations use it in places their broadcast vans can't reach.''

\textit{I did not quite understand it, but it sounded impressive.}

``So they're going to use that to livestream our suffering in high definition?''

``That's what Hijiri-kun said.''

\textit{The bastard---if he knew that, he could have told me too instead of just Kannabi, I thought, but technically I was the enemy. Even Hijiri, with all his noble airs, would not go out of his way to help an enemy.}

\textit{Even though he was a humanities student, the thought of competing against someone that flawless was terrifying.}

For a while after that, the two of us pored over physics and math books. Sometimes we were absorbed in different books, and other times we worked together, sharing interesting topics or looking up things we were both curious about. \textit{It went much faster than reading by myself.}

Iwama had said she was weak in physics, but I did not feel that way at all. If anything, she seemed fairly knowledgeable. As expected from a former Science Club member. It made perfect sense that she would bring up dilatancy while talking about potato starch. Even in topics that were Greek to me, she had at least a basic grasp of them and explained them clearly in her own words.

This might have been the first time I had ever felt that studying with someone else could be this much fun.

$\gamma$

Becoming talkative when I was nervous was a bad habit of mine.

But since it felt like I would only make awkward gestures if I stayed quiet, I decided to make full use of that bad habit. When I talked about science, my brain focused its resources there. That way, I could avoid being too aware of the person right next to me, listening so intently to my explanation.

\textit{I was certain the way I was now was abnormal.}

\textit{Thinking so much about the person next to me even though I had come here to study was, frankly, close to an illness. It felt like the same kind of suffering as being unable to bring down a painful fever. It felt as if my mental immune cells were releasing massive amounts of cytokines.}

\textit{If there were a treatment that could at least relieve the symptoms of this feeling, I would try it without hesitation. I was clearly unable to control it, and there was no sign that it would subside. If anything, I felt like it was only getting worse.}

\textit{Would it get a little better if I could at least identify the pathogen?}

Thinking that in the back of my mind, I launched into an explanation of electronic tinkering.

``For the transistor's three legs, if you turn the flat side of the head toward you, then from the left they're emitter, collector, and base. You remember it as ECB, like `ekubo.'''

``Since `emit' means to release and `collect' means to gather, is it right to assume that when voltage is applied to the base, current flows from the emitter to the collector?''

``Oh, this diagram is the NPN type, so strictly speaking, it's the electrons that flow.''

``I see, so the current goes in the opposite direction.''

There was a rumor that engineering themes came up every year in Science Club Ori, so I was explaining basic electronic components to Del-chan while opening an introductory book full of photos. Even when I pointed out his mistakes, Del-chan did not look annoyed and took it all in right away. It was easy when I did not have to worry about that.

``The person who defined pi using the diameter instead of the radius, and the person who made the direction of current opposite to the flow of electrons, must surely be regretting it in heaven by now.''

``If God is omniscient and omnipotent, those people are probably in hell, if anything.''

Del-chan was definitely the sort of person who was naturally sharp-tongued. That was what I had thought while listening to him talk with Mizasaki-kun. Perhaps he had been holding back out of consideration during the spring, because he had not shown that sharp tongue very much in his conversations with me. But now, he was comfortable having the same kind of conversations with me that he had with Mizasaki-kun. \textit{It made me a little happy.}

``Oh, I might have the real thing here.''

``The real thing? You mean a transistor?''

``Yeah. Just in case, I've got one buried deep in my bag...''

\textit{In truth, I had put it in my bag because I thought I might end up talking about electronic tinkering with Del-chan, but I was not about to admit that.} I took a zippered pouch out of my bag, and from it pulled out the transistor, a button battery, and a red LED. The transistor was a component with three thin legs sticking out of a piece of black plastic about the size of a morning glory seed. The LED was about the same size, with two legs extending from the bulb. The longer one was the anode on the positive side, and the shorter one was the cathode on the negative side.

``Are you saying you carry all of that around in your bag just in case?''

\textit{I could not possibly tell him that I had brought it for the one person who listened to me talk about science more seriously than anyone else.}

I assembled the circuit in my hands. I spread the three legs of the transistor and twisted the collector together with the cathode of the LED. It was complete once I held it so the button cell battery was sandwiched between the emitter and the LED's anode.

``Right now, there's one transistor leg left over. That's the base. If even the tiniest electrical connection is made between it and the emitter, electricity will flow to the LED.''

``The emitter is hidden by your finger, Iwama, but...''

``It does not necessarily have to be connected by metal. Water---or even the human body---works too.''

I touched the base with a finger on the hand that was not holding the transistor.

In that instant, the LED glowed red. It went out the moment I removed my finger. As I repeated it several times, the LED blinked in time with my movements.

``Controlling the current between the collector and emitter like this based on the state of the base is the basic function of a transistor. It acts as a switch or an amplifier.''

``I see. How weak can that electrical connection be?''

``I think it would probably still work even with two people in between. Try holding this base part for a second. And then---''

Once I had said that much, I realized I was in the middle of suggesting something outrageous. \textit{My mother was usually the one who helped with experiments like this, so I had forgotten.}

``And then?''

Del-chan had already pinched the base, so there was no turning back. All that was left was for the emitter hidden under my finger and the base Del-chan was touching to be electrically connected through the two of us.

What that meant, in other words, was---

``Um, if you do not mind, that is.''

Adding that disclaimer, I held out my free hand in front of Del-chan.

``Could you try touching this hand with the one that is not holding the base?''

Del-chan hesitated for a moment, then gently touched my palm with his index finger. The LED did not light up.

``Oh, that's strange...''

``Should I touch it a little more firmly?''

``Eh? Ah, yeah. Maybe.''

\textit{This might have been the first time we had been in a situation like this since Golden Week. Del-chan did not seem particularly shaken, but I felt like I was already at my limit. Do your best, my parasympathetic nervous system.}

Del-chan's palm was gently laid over mine.

It felt as though a real electric shock had surged through me. The LED glowed bright red, and I accidentally let go. The button cell battery fell to the floor and rolled away.

``Ah! Sorry!''

\textit{It was, in fact, a convenient accident. I took my time picking up the button cell battery to let my heart rate settle.}

By the time I looked up again, I had already come up with a way to escape the atmosphere.

``Hey, working here is a bit... you know, so why don't we move somewhere else?''

$\delta$

At Iwama's suggestion, we moved to the cafe attached to the library and sat at an outdoor terrace table. The sunlight was as brutal as ever, but a large parasol was doing its job, and the summer breeze felt pleasant. Thanks in part to a mist sprayer some distance away, it did not feel that hot.

The suggestion to change locations saved me. Iwama did not seem to mind at all, but the feeling of touching her hand had brought back that night during Golden Week. \textit{I was sure the agitation had shown on my face.} I somehow managed to hide it by pretending to focus on packing up my things to move.

Incidentally, I later learned that this transistor-based mechanism had once been commercialized under the ominous name ``Love Tester.'' Iwama probably did not know that.

But I just could not calm my nerves. ``My mind was elsewhere'' felt like the perfect phrase for it.

Sitting across from me, Iwama was happily reading the menu. \textit{I was sure she was completely unaware that she had nearly killed a naive boy. To Iwama, holding a classmate's hand was probably an everyday act, like grabbing a strap on a train. But for me, it was different.}

``Since we're here, maybe I'll have a parfait.''

I also put on a composed front in front of Iwama, who was saying such carefree things.

``Right. If we're staying here until tonight, I'll probably get hungry.''

While we were being shown to our seats by the waiter, I had noticed someone eating a peach parfait, and it had caught my eye. It was topped with a whole white peach cut into fine wedges. \textit{I like fruit in general about as much as I like cherry tomatoes. I eat cherry tomatoes all year round, but whenever seasonal fruits start showing up in stores, I always jump on them.}

Strawberries and cherries in spring; melons, watermelons, peaches, and blueberries in summer; grapes, pears, and persimmons in autumn; and apples and mandarins all through winter. \textit{Not only do I eat them as they are, but if I have the money to spare, I make it a rule to try sweets made with whatever fruit is in season.}

But there was a problem. I could not find the peach parfait on the menu. Before I could figure out what was going on, the waiter from earlier brought the water and pressed me for my order with a rushed ``May I take your order?'', so I ended up ordering the chocolate banana parfait and iced coffee that happened to catch my eye.

``I'll have the Setouchi lemon iced tea and the peach parfait, please!''

In front of Iwama---who ordered with one of her bright, sparkling smiles---lay a laminated menu. It was different from the pamphlet-style one made of folded cardstock that I had. \textit{I realized what had happened, but it was already too late; the waiter was already heading back with an annoyed look on his face.}

The waiter looked about college age, and the nametag on his chest had a beginner's mark on it. Perhaps because he was inexperienced, he had given Iwama and me different kinds of menus. As a result, we had inadvertently ended up with the only copy of both the regular menu and the seasonal menu.

\textit{Still, it was not worth the trouble of calling back that halfhearted waiter just to change my order. I did not want to see Iwama look apologetic over something as small as not sharing the menu, either.}

By the way, the menus the waiter had brought us---in other words, the ones he was supposed to take back---were still sitting on our table. If they stayed there, Iwama might notice the blunder we had made.

``Wow! You can't see the ocean from here, but the atmosphere is great with all this forest around us!''

Just then, as if on cue, Iwama started taking a photo of the scenery with her smartphone. I took that chance to stack the two menus and, since there was nowhere else nearby to hide them, placed them on my lap. Fortunately, when Iwama turned back, she seemed to have forgotten the menus had ever been on the table at all. \textit{If she had noticed, she was the sort of person who would definitely have been considerate enough to ask, ``Oh, did you put the menus away?'' If anything, I was probably the strange one for agonizing over a bit of cardstock, but better safe than sorry.}

Just as I was feeling relieved, Iwama suddenly asked me.

``By the way, Del-chan, were you really okay with not getting the peach parfait?''

``...Huh?''

Her timing was so perfect it was like she'd read my mind, and I couldn't even get out a proper reply.

``Mizasaki-kun mentioned it once. He said Del-chan has a weakness for seasonal fruit, and especially loves white peaches.''

\textit{So that was it. That guy had been spraying my personal information everywhere like a lawn sprinkler.}

``Actually, I had more than enough peaches yesterday. Today, I'm in a banana kind of mood.''

``I see!''

\textit{It was a lie, but she bought it. Now all I had to do was keep the menu I'd dropped onto my lap hidden until we left the table. Unless she crouched all the way down to look under the table or came around to my side, Iwama was never going to see it.}

The iced coffee and iced tea showed up quickly, long before the parfait did. When Iwama dipped a lemon slice into it, the tea shifted from reddish-brown to orange. It was the lemon's acidity neutralizing the reddish theaflavins in the tea.

I was just about to stop myself, thinking it'd be too weird to bring that up in a café, when Iwama set her bag on her lap and started pulling out electronic components. \textit{There was someone even weirder than me.}

``Since we're out, there's something else I wanted to show you besides the transistors.''

As she spoke, Iwama placed a small component on her palm that looked almost identical to a transistor.

``What's that?''

``A melody IC. It makes electronic sounds by turning changes in current into vibrations. Did you ever see one in tech class or something?''

After thinking about it for a moment, I shook my head. I hadn't taken tech class very seriously, so I barely remembered any of it. I might have seen one before, but I clearly hadn't bothered storing that in my hippocampus. I did, however, vaguely remember arguing with Mizasaki about the melting point of lead-free solder.

Iwama connected a small speaker about the size of a five-hundred-yen coin to two of the three legs sprouting from the melody IC, and then wedged a button battery into the remaining gap.

``Pi-ro-ri-ro-ri''---an electronic rendition of ``Für Elise'' played, for lack of a better way to describe the sound. \textit{I see; this was why it'd been hard to demonstrate it in the library.}

``So you only need this, a power source, and a speaker to play music.''

``Exactly! And you see...''

It didn't look impressive enough to deserve a special demonstration, but Iwama still seemed to have something up her sleeve. \textit{It wouldn't be good if the conversation circled back to peaches.} I kept my eyes on Iwama's hands and made sure not to do anything unnecessary. Her slender fingers moved without hesitation. She disconnected the speaker and connected the LED she'd used earlier in its place. Seen from the side, the way she connected the parts just by twisting them together without even using solder was practically craftsmanship.

When she finally wedged the button battery back in, the current that had been running to the speaker flowed into the LED instead, so naturally, it lit up. It looked like it was flickering faintly.

``Del-chan, do you know a way to `hear' this light?''

``Hear light...?''

``Yeah. I have various other parts here, so feel free to use them.''

There was still no sign of the parfait. They were probably harvesting the peaches right now. Iwama cheerfully lined up a length of fishing line thick enough to haul in a tuna, some small mirrors, and a small solar cell on the table. I thought it through.

``Of the things Iwama added, there's only one other electronic component: that solar cell. If we connect that to the speaker and it receives the light from the LED, what happens?''

``I'll try it!''

Iwama immediately connected the speaker she'd just removed to the solar cell. The solar cell should have been generating electricity from the summer sunlight, but since that light carried no musical information, of course it made no sound.

Then, when she brought the LED connected to the melody IC close to it---

``Oh, I hear it.''

Out came a tinny ``Für Elise.'' The LED's flickering became fluctuations in the solar cell's power output, which then vibrated the speaker and turned into sound. \textit{It might have seemed obvious if I'd sat down and thought through the logic, but seeing it happen right in front of me still felt strangely mysterious. To think you could pull off wireless communication with something this simple.}

``Actually, one turns electricity into light and the other turns light into electricity, but LEDs and solar cells both rely on very similar mechanisms involving a semiconductor pn-junction.''

Iwama explained as she pointed to the electronic components she'd just thrown together on the spot.

\fullpageimage{4.jpg}

\thispagestyle{plain}
\vspace{1cm}

``It's like how motors and generators have the same structure using magnets and coils.''

``Exactly! Motors and generators are conversions between electricity and rotation, but LEDs and solar cells are like the electricity and light versions. Converting information into light for transmission and reception like this is the basic mechanism of optical communication. Light only travels in a straight line, but if you use the things I have here...''

At that moment, Iwama's hands came to a sudden halt. A male customer had been guided to a table near us. But he didn't seem to care about the suspicious pair with electronic components spread all over their table. I called out to the back of the lazy waiter, who was about to leave after taking nothing but an order for iced coffee. ``Um, could you take the menus with you?''

Just as I'd feared, \textit{this is bad}, Iwama's gaze was already on the waiter.

``Oh? Come to think of it, what happened to our menus?''

To make matters worse, Iwama's eyes began searching the floor around us. A breeze occasionally blew through this terrace seating. She was probably worried the menus had blown away while she was taking pictures of the scenery.

\textit{But the menus were sitting on my lap. I considered saying, ``They're right here,'' and putting them on the table. But if she noticed there were two different menus, Iwama might figure out the real reason I hadn't ordered the peach parfait. To keep that from happening, I had no choice but to keep them hidden.}

``Didn't the waiter take them?''

At the lie I came up with on the spot, relief spread across Iwama's face, and she stopped looking.

``I guess so, you're right. Thank goodness!''

``I thought they'd flown away while I wasn't looking.''

``I would've noticed if they were flying.''

\textit{I hated keeping up a lie I wasn't used to telling, but once I'd started, there was no helping it.}

\textit{It was fine for now. As long as Iwama didn't lean into some odd position and notice the menus on my lap, it wouldn't be a problem.}

``To get back to the topic, you can bend light if you use a mirror, right?''

When I steered the conversation back to optical communication, Iwama nodded happily.

``Right! By the way, what do you think the fishing line is for?''

Then, after showing that the music could still be heard perfectly fine even when reflected off a mirror, Iwama aimed the LED light at one end of the thick fishing line. When she did, red light spilled out from the other end of the line.

``I see, it's an optical fiber.''

``Exactly! Because the light keeps undergoing total internal reflection inside the fishing line, it barely loses any strength...''

When she aimed the light that had passed through the fishing line at the solar cell, ``Für Elise'' played faintly.

Having finished trying out the full suite of optical communication demonstration experiments, Iwama began packing up the components. \textit{The peaches they were harvesting ought to have reached the kitchen by now. At this time of year, the skin of a good peach would peel right off}---and while I was busy thinking such foolish thoughts, I noticed Iwama moving strangely.

She lowered her gaze to her lap, went still, and seemed to be thinking hard about something. Her smartphone had been sitting on the table ever since she'd taken the landscape photos. So what exactly was she looking at?

Perhaps noticing me watching her, Iwama immediately resumed packing.

``Oh right, that mirror experiment from earlier has an interesting application!''

The science talk started right back up. According to Iwama, if you reflect a laser off a windowpane, the light will vibrate along with the window itself---in other words, with the vibrations of the air inside the room---making it possible to eavesdrop from outside. But window glass doesn't vibrate that easily, so when she tried it at home, she could apparently only barely make out a blast of death metal.

I couldn't hold back a laugh at the image of death metal blasting through Iwama's room.

``But the heavy bass is great! Lower sounds make the glass shake more easily.''

As she said that, Iwama broke into a grin too. The sight of her trying to justify herself while holding back a laugh too big to stay just a smile was so funny my abs were about to cramp, right as the parfaits arrived.

This was my first time ordering a parfait at this library (which was only natural, since a library is a place for reading books), but what arrived was far more magnificent than I'd imagined. The choco-banana parfait had white soft-serve piled at the top, with bananas arranged around it like a blooming flower. The surface of the bananas was a toasted caramel brown. Black and white chocolate sauce was drizzled over it with the kind of cute flair that called to mind the chocolate bananas sold at festival stalls.

The peach parfait set in front of Iwama had a whole peach perched on top in such a delicate balance that it looked ready to tumble off, and it was garnished with a generous amount of translucent green spearmint. Peaches and mint are an outstanding match. It wasn't just there for decoration; it was meant to be eaten with the peach.

``Wow! Can I take a picture?''

I nodded and said, ``Of course,'' to Iwama, who asked while pointing at her phone on the table. Figuring I might as well take one too, I picked up my phone.

Parfaits are generally served in tall glasses, and in elaborate shops, beautiful layers are visible on the sides of those glasses. The parfait here was no exception. When I tried taking one from an angle that showed the side, the peach parfait across from me and even Iwama's chest ended up in the frame. My finger stopped before I could delete it, thinking \textit{it wouldn't be good if my younger sister saw it}, and on impulse, I took another photo from directly above.

``Oh, this kind of makes it look like I'm `hinting'...''

Whether she was having the same problem or not, Iwama murmured that as she moved her phone slightly up and down.

``What do you mean by `hinting'?''

``Ah, no, it's nothing. Well, whatever. Del-chan, say cheese!''

She pointed the camera at me just once. It seemed she'd decided that if I was going to end up partly in the frame anyway, she might as well just take a picture of me. I left my hand on the table in a peace sign, but when she showed me the photo, I looked like I was negotiating prices in a black-market deal.

The photo session ended, and we dug into the parfaits before the summer breeze could melt the ice cream. The choco-banana parfait was overly sweet, but that paired well with the bitter iced coffee. The soft banana felt warm on a tongue cooled by the soft-serve. The syrupy sweetness of the chocolate sauce tied everything together. \textit{This might have been the first time I'd ever had such a sweet summer.}

Iwama seemed to be going with a strategy of eating the sorbet under the peach first to stabilize the top-heavy structure. From what I could see, most of the fruit, which had been cut into twelve wedges, still remained. \textit{It looked delicious. Just from accidentally looking at it, I ended up imagining the taste. An artful white peach, with peach-colored highlights on its plump white flesh. There was no way it could be anything but good. If I'd been here with Mizasaki, I would've stolen a bite, but the person sitting across from me was Iwama. The forbidden fruit. That beautiful peach, certain to burst with juice the moment you bit into it, could never become sour grapes.}

``Del-chan, do you want some?''

By the time I noticed, it was already too late. \textit{Apparently even an outside observer could tell my eyes had been drawn to the peach.}

``...No, not really.''

``Really? This peach is so good.''

She slid the now-stable parfait toward me. I supposed that meant I could have some. But the line I'd given was that I'd had my fill of peaches yesterday and was in a banana mood today. Besides, eating from a parfait Iwama had already started was, well, how should I put it... not great for my mental state. That was coming from someone who got flustered just from their hands touching. There was no telling what kind of emotion might burst out of me.

``Thanks. I'll appreciate the offer.''

Iwama wasn't the kind of person to keep pushing at a time like this. So I thought she'd back off, but Iwama went and did something outrageous.

She placed a slice of peach on her spoon and held it out to me. \textit{Even I knew this was what people called ``Aah-hn,'' the kind of thing lovey-dovey couples did.}

``Here, eat up. It looks like it's going to fall if you don't hurry.''

Iwama had a fork too, but if she'd deliberately chosen the unstable spoon just so she could say that, she was a formidable tactician. I couldn't just let a precious white peach end up eaten by the table, so I was forced to take a bite of the falling peach.

``Is it good?''

I focused on it and savored it as I chewed, and after taking in the balance of aroma, sweetness, and acidity, I nodded.

``It's incredibly delicious. It's fruit in its final form, optimized for the human tongue.''

``Then have another bite! Under the peach, there's some really good sherbet.''

From that point on, I was at Iwama's mercy.

Even though we weren't a couple, I ended up eating from the parfait in Iwama's hand several times, and in return, I shared a few bites of mine.

But why had Iwama been so insistent on making me eat the peach parfait?

$\gamma$

\textit{That person had no intention of ever forgetting what Izuta Sho and Hijiri Mitsuyuki had done over Golden Week.}

\textit{I finally found him.}

\textit{The boy I was looking for was sitting a short distance away with a woman, sharing a parfait. They looked quite intimate,} that person thought, a smirk playing at their lips.

\textit{Today was the perfect chance. It was a little inconvenient that they were together, but if they were in the library, there would undoubtedly be moments when both of them stepped away from their seats for a while. It wasn't as though they kept their belongings on them at all times.}

\textit{That person intended to leave Izuta Sho a modest gift.}

$\gamma$

As I headed back to the lounge area, I was conducting an intense self-review in my head.

\textit{Feeding someone a parfait with an `aah-hn' when we aren't even dating `yet' is insane. No, what do I mean by `yet'? It's not like there were any plans for us to date in the future. It was the wrong adverb to use.}

\textit{Of course, I knew I'd crossed the bounds of common sense the moment I started fiddling with electronics in a café, but at least that could still be justified as a way to distract from the tension.}

\textit{But the `aah-hn' was too much.}

\textit{To justify myself, I felt like I had to make him eat the peach. Because I'd made a serious mistake.}

\textit{I first realized something was off when the man at the next table asked the waiter to take the menus. That waiter, with the beginner's badge on his chest, was clearly inexperienced. I was pretty sure that with us too, he'd gone back right after taking our order without collecting the menus from the table. Del-chan said, `the waiter took them,' but...}

\textit{If the waiter hadn't taken them away, then where had our menus disappeared to?}

\textit{Because I was nervous, I hadn't been paying attention to what was on the table before or after we ordered. So I couldn't be confident in my memory, but I couldn't stop wondering where the menus had gone.}

\textit{And with a small trick, I found them.}

\textit{Del-chan had been hiding the menus on his lap the whole time. In other words, Del-chan had been lying. There was no way the waiter had taken them. Because Del-chan had them.}

\textit{Then why had he done that? Why did he need to tell a lie like that? There was plenty of space on the table. Unless he had a particular reason, he should have just set them at the edge of the table. In other words, there was a reason. A reason he wanted to keep the menus hidden, even if it meant lying.}

\textit{He didn't want me to see them.}

\textit{Stacked on Del-chan's lap were the booklet menu and the laminated menu I'd been reading. One of each. That's when it hit me.}

\textit{I hadn't shown Del-chan the summer-limited menu. Del-chan had wanted to order the peach parfait. But I'd kept the menu with the peach parfait on it by my side the whole time. Because of that, he couldn't order his favorite peach parfait. And Del-chan had hidden the menu so I wouldn't notice.}

\textit{Judging by the serious look in his eyes when he saw the peach, Del-chan being `strictly in the mood for bananas' was a little suspicious. More than anything, the fact that he'd hidden the menu and then tried to cover it up with a lie was proof that he was keeping his desire to `eat the peach parfait' a secret.}

\textit{To think I'd made him worry that much!}

\textit{That's why I had to make Del-chan eat the peach no matter what. I thought he might eat it if I handed him the whole glass, but things don't work out that smoothly in real life.}

\textit{But still, the `aah-hn' was too much! The sense of distance between us is definitely way too weird!}

Before heading back into the quiet interior of the building, I apologized just in case.

``Sorry about earlier, for doing something strange.''

``Something strange?''

Del-chan echoed in his usual tone. \textit{Maybe for Del-chan, who has a younger sister (and is apparently lovey-dovey with her, according to rumors), an `aah-hn' was just an everyday occurrence.}

``You know, starting to do optical communication and stuff even though it's technically a café...''

``It was interesting, and you don't have anything to apologize for. I doubt the other customers cared anyway.''

``You think so...?''

``If you'd started blasting death metal, I would've stopped you, though.''

``We promised to forget about that!''

At my protest, Del-chan laughed in delight.

``We are kindred spirits. There's no need to hold back when talking about the things we like. I won't hold back either, and I like listening to you talk about the things you like, Iwama.''

Del-chan said it so casually. \textit{How many times had the word `like' appeared in that sentence? Three times? No, maybe four if you counted `kindred spirits.'}

\textit{Even though this wasn't like me at all, my heart wouldn't stop pounding.}

\textit{I know what set all this off.}
