When I asked, Togakushi-kun flipped the paper over. There was only one sentence there.

\begin{center}
\textit{Search for the path, relying on the voice that cannot be heard.}
\end{center}

``\dots\dots What's that supposed to mean?''

``Who knows. Not from this alone. It might be connected to a later task.''

Deciding to check the items that had been left there for the time being, we headed toward the scaffold.

The Kannon Caves were a cavern opening in a cliff along the river. Apparently, a Kannon statue was enshrined deep inside. A path had been cut into the cliff leading to the cave entrance. Beyond that, a temporary scaffold had been set up.

It was a metal scaffold like the kind you see at construction sites. It was basically a short bridge, and it looked more than a little flimsy. It had been built along the cliff and connected to a foothold just beyond it. It looked like you could climb to the top of the cliff from beyond that foothold.

It was pretty high up, about seven or eight meters above the river. Large rocks were scattered all through the river below, and it definitely looked dangerous to fall. That applied not just to E-mi, but to us too.

``Wait a moment.''

Hijiri-kun put a firm stop to Togakushi-kun, who was trying to hurry ahead.

``Supposedly, in the `Science Dept Orientation Murder Incident,' someone died right here on this scaffold. We can't be too careful.''

Hijiri-kun pushed his way to the front. He moved slowly along the path to the Kannon Caves, and when he reached the scaffold, he stomped on it several times to test how safe it was.

``Hurry up.''

``I'm the type who taps on a stone bridge over and over before crossing it.''

Saying that, Hijiri-kun even started jumping on the scaffold while holding onto the rocks along the cliff face. Once he seemed satisfied with how sturdy it was, he crossed to the other side and held out a hand to help Togakushi-kun across. Then he reached a hand out to me. When I glanced over, I saw the camera crew filming us from the road on the other side of the river. \textit{Thinking I really didn't want Del-chan to see this,} I crossed the scaffold with Hijiri-kun's help.

We scrambled up the rocky slope and came to a small clearing, where we found it.

``You've got to be kidding.''

I felt a flicker of sympathy at the sheer exasperation in Togakushi-kun's voice.

$\delta$

``We really should've gone to the station first.''

``Shut up. We're already on our way, so don't complain.''

The three of us were sitting side by side in the back row of the bus. In order, it was me, Watari-kun, and Mikage. Maybe they hadn't gotten permission to film inside the bus, because the two camera crew members sitting two rows ahead had stopped recording.

I remembered sitting in these same seats on a bus on this same route once before. It was back at the start of the school year, on the day Iwama and I had ended up at Koshizaki Station. Unlike then, my heart felt restless now.

After finishing the problems, we'd argued outside the school about where to head first, but once we realized we wouldn't be able to catch a bus to the farthest point, the Kannon Caves, for a while, we'd planned to go to the station instead. But then a bus bound for Koshizaki Station showed up right then, and we jumped on it based on Watari-kun's instincts. It looked like this bus could get us to the Koshizaki Tunnel, one of our destinations. However---

``If we go to the tunnel first and then miss the bus to the Kannon Caves, what exactly are you planning to do? We could've decided the order for the three locations after checking the timetable at the station.''

``What do you mean, `what'? We'll deal with it when we get there. What do you have legs for?''

``Don't tell me you're planning to run.''

``Is running forbidden in orienteering?''

``Even with Mikage with us?''

``No, it's fine. I can run plenty too.''

Mikage, who had been watching our stupid argument with amusement, said it mildly.

Watari-kun's gaze flicked across her chest as if he'd only just noticed, then dropped to the floor of the aisle.

``\dots\dots Well, it definitely wasn't planned. Let's think of a way to avoid running as much as possible.''

``Don't worry. I'm wearing battle-ready underwear today.''

While I was still doubting my ears after that whispered comment, Mikage corrected herself.

``My mistake. Contest underwear.''

``What's `contest underwear'? Are you planning to get into a fight or something?''

``No. When moving, the Coope---''

``Don't say another word.''

I cut Mikage off in a hurry. \textit{I was sure she was trying to be considerate by opening up to us, but it was way too candid.}

``There aren't any transit IC cards or taxi tickets in there, right?''

When I checked with Watari-kun, who was clutching the backpack, he nodded as he checked inside.

``Other than the one-day bus pass, there's nothing in here that looks useful for getting around. The lunch money is just city meal vouchers that only work at restaurants. It'd be nice if one of the restaurants also rented out bicycles or something.''

\textit{Well, even if they did, using one really would be a last resort.}

There were other items in the backpack that I didn't really understand. There were the transit pass holders the three of us had already split up, and the G-Shock wristwatch that Watari-kun had ended up wearing on the team's behalf. The map and compass made sense. But there was also a pair of sunglasses, a small LED flashlight, a portable radio, and a single ceramic earphone plugged into it. I had no idea what any of them were for.

Watari-kun inspected each item with a grim look before letting out a sigh.

``Good grief, that sadistic Game Master woman... what is she planning to make us do? She's tossing us out into the city with nothing but this junk, and she didn't even give us an intro or a tutorial.''

Mikage turned toward us, looking oddly pleased by the phrase ``sadistic woman.''

``Apparently, they give the selected members some pretty reckless, unreasonable tasks every year.''

``So there really is a chance we'll end up having to run?''

Mikage nodded at my bitter expression.

``Yes. Be prepared. Back when my older brother did it---and the difficulty was downright demonic---Karato-senpai nearly got heatstroke. I'm sure that sadistic woman will be more than happy to carry on that tradition.''

Karato-senpai was the only current third-year biology club member. If each club had to send a first-year, then she must've been forced to take part whether she wanted to or not. \textit{I felt awful for her.}

``She must really enjoy messing with her juniors. She even pulled a narrative trick on us before we even started, and she was smirking while watching our reactions.''

``What's a narrative trick?''

``You don't even know that? It's a cheap move where you leave out key information while explaining things so the other person gets led into a misunderstanding. In novels, it's used to deliberately trick readers about a character's true identity because they can't see their face.''

Come to think of it, I might've read a mystery like that before.

``Like assuming someone is a refined lady because she ends her sentences with `desu-wa,' only to find out she's actually a middle-aged guy from Kansai.''

I didn't really follow Mikage's explanation. \textit{In the first place, did refined ladies really end their sentences with ``desu-wa''?}

``Hongo-senpai's narrative trick was the team split. She told us she was picking members from three clubs for a three-way battle, which made us think it'd be club versus club, but in reality she put us into teams none of us had expected.''

``Exactly. It was dirty. If we're not careful, we might get tricked again.''

\textit{It felt like my brain would wear out if I stayed suspicious of every single thing.} I decided to leave the role of skeptic to Watari-kun.

The bus rolled through the city center and into a quiet residential area. From inside the air-conditioned bus, the sky was a stirring shade of blue, but the moment we stepped off, it would probably turn into a blazing hell. \textit{If we ended up having to run around outside... I didn't even want to think about it.}

``I wonder where Team Y is right now.''

When I muttered that, Watari-kun furrowed his brow.

``You saw the timetable. If those guys moved at top speed, it's possible they caught the 10:08 bus and have already made it to the Kannon Caves. Though that might be overthinking it.''

After saying that in an intentionally loud voice, Watari-kun looked toward the camera crew. The two athletic-looking second-years watching us from two rows ahead clearly didn't have very good poker faces. \textit{The mean little smirks on their faces were the kind people wore when they were deliberately not pointing out someone else's mistake.} They each had an earphone in one ear, so they must've been getting information through them.

\textit{Team Y had already made it to the Kannon Caves.}

Catching a bus five minutes after finishing the problems was insanely tight, but not impossible. If someone had the timetable memorized, they could've pulled it off.

Watari-kun lowered his voice until it was almost swallowed by the sound of the bus and whispered to Mikage and me.

``I dug around a little... that Togakushi from Team Y is a pro gamer who even enters international tournaments. He's even posted good results in domestic RTA tournaments.''

``RTA... that means Real Time Attack, right...?''

``Did you think it was related to RNA, Biology Boy? Yeah, it's a time attack. Togakushi's an efficiency freak even among efficiency freaks. Apparently, he always falls asleep face-down on his desk less than twenty minutes after a test starts. That's what Fudo told me. And he solved that test in three minutes today too. We're up against a monster like that.''

I was speechless.

Every subject in the recent finals had a sixty-minute time limit. \textit{To think he'd solved those tests, where he'd scored over 90\% across five subjects, in about a third of the allotted time.}

Mikage peered into my stunned face.

``All we can do is our best in our own way. If we panic and lose our rhythm, it'll only backfire.''

``...You're right. Especially today, all we need to do is make it back within the time limit.''

``Yeah. Even if we lose in the end, I'm definitely not going to let them see me looking pathetic.''

Staring straight ahead toward the front of the bus, Watari-kun pressed the stop button firmly with the knuckle of his index finger.

The moment we stepped off the bus, harsh sunlight came pouring down on us. Watari-kun, that guy, had taken it upon himself to wear the single pair of sunglasses we'd been given. \textit{It was irritating how well they actually suited his stern, long-haired face.} Watari-kun looked at the G-Shock on his right wrist, then tilted his head from side to side several times for some reason.

``What are you doing?''

``No, it's nothing. It's 10:46 now. Assuming the bus ride took fifteen minutes, I want us done here within an hour at the absolute latest. If we make it to the station by 12:00, we can take lunch as early as possible.''

``I'll remember the return bus.''

After saying that, Mikage immediately crossed to the other side of the road.

``The tunnel's definitely a little farther ahead, right?''

``Yeah. There's a cape over there. The bus shouldn't have gone through the tunnel yet. I'm pretty sure the bus to Koshizaki Station goes through that tunnel once. That should be the Koshizaki Tunnel.''

``Did you go to Koshizaki? There's nothing out there.''

``Is going somewhere forbidden just because there's nothing there?''

``I didn't say it was forbidden. I was asking out of pure curiosity, you weird glasses-wearing bastard.''

\textit{I couldn't exactly tell him I'd gone there to look at the sea with Iwama.}

``There's one leaving in an hour, at 11:45. If we take that, we should get to the station at 12:00.''

``Nice. Then let's walk.''

Watari-kun took the lead, and we walked in single file just outside the white line at the edge of the road.

After we'd walked for a while, the tunnel came into view just as I'd expected.

The problem was that there was nothing there. To begin with, cars were constantly coming and going through the tunnel, and the sidewalks were narrow; it didn't look like the kind of place where you could do anything except pass through.

I checked the map in a cold sweat, wondering if we were really on the right track. I had folded it and drawn a straight line to confirm it, so there was no mistake---the destination definitely pointed to the area around this tunnel. My decoding of the DNA should have been correct too. \textit{If I was going to trust my own work, there had to be something around here.}

``I sometimes come this way on walks, and I'm pretty sure there's another tunnel above this one,'' Mikage said. ``It's called the Koshizaki Tunnel, and it's for pedestrians only.''

``That's it. Do you know how to get there?''

Mikage nodded at my question with complete confidence on her face.

``Probably.''

\textit{Which is it?}

When we entered the residential area beside the tunnel, we did indeed find a narrow path leading up toward the ridge. With Mikage leading the way, we made our way up a slope I would never normally have set foot on. By the time we were all dripping with sweat, our destination finally came into view. It was a small tunnel carved directly into the rock, low enough that a tall person would probably have to stoop to get through. It was clearly a passage used only by the locals.

I was sure this had to be the checkpoint. A sunshade tarp had been set up in front of the tunnel, and under it a group of student organizers and another camera crew were waiting. There was a table set up with a water jug that probably held drinks, along with some salt candy. \textit{The tension drained out of me in a rush of relief.}

But there was no warm welcome waiting for us. The moment we arrived, a senior student thrust a board with instructions written on it right in front of us.

This tunnel has been turned into a ``certain table'' familiar to all you science students. Select only one of the following two and place it in the matching grid space.

$\cdot$ Metallic Element Alpha

$\cdot$ Metallic Element Beta

Only the item attached to that grid space will serve as the investigative material for your clue.

※ Two identical sets of investigative materials have been prepared (front and back). Please take only one.

\begin{flushright}
Great Detective Hongo Nagisa
\end{flushright}

Under the labels for Alpha and Beta, two different metals had been set into the board. The three of us leaned in for a closer look.

Alpha was a silvery-white liquid metal sealed inside a test tube with a rubber stopper. Beta was a cluster of metallic crystals that looked like a glitching jumble of overlapping shapes, its surface shimmering with an oily rainbow sheen.

``Beta is bismuth,'' I said. ``Alpha is either mercury or gallium, but at this temperature I can't tell which one it is.''

\textit{Strictly speaking, there are other liquid metals, but cesium and rubidium, for example, react so easily with water and air that they'd catch fire, and putting them in a test tube like this would be a safety nightmare. Francium, meanwhile, is radioactive and exists on Earth in quantities of only a few dozen grams.}

As I was saying that, Watari immediately reached out.

``Then let's go with the one we know for sure: bismuth.''

``Wait.''

I instinctively grabbed Watari's arm to stop him. He glared at me, and I hurried to explain myself.

``Can we take a look inside the tunnel first? If it's what I think it is, bismuth might be kind of inconvenient.''

``What the hell is that supposed to mean?''

Ignoring Watari's protests, I stepped into the tunnel. Between the shade and the cold stone, the air inside felt a little chilly. And just as I'd expected, the metal reinforcing the walls formed a grid pattern. A long row of Ziploc bags had been attached to it, each containing a piece of paper. In some places, bags had been replaced with other objects---spray cans of hydrogen or helium gas, a one-yen coin, or suspicious yellow-green glowing beads.

``It's the periodic table,'' Mikage said, counting the grid spaces. There were sixteen empty spaces between the hydrogen and helium cans. This was unmistakably a recreation of the periodic table you'd find in any chemistry textbook.

As the name suggests, the periodic table is a chart that lists the many elements that make up the world---such as hydrogen (H) and aluminum (Al)---in order of their atomic numbers. It makes use of the fact that the properties of elements change periodically; for example, the far-right column is made up entirely of low-reactivity gases called ``noble gases,'' like helium and neon. The table is arranged so that elements with similar properties line up in vertical columns.

That entire system had been recreated here using the tunnel's grid-like walls.

``Hey, Delta, what are these weird-colored beads?'' Watari asked.

``Uranium glass. It's glass with a trace amount of sodium diuranate mixed into it, and it fluoresces yellow-green when exposed to ultraviolet light. If you count the hydrogen row as the first, it goes right there in the ninth row.''

``Just what I'd expect from Delta-kun,'' Mikage said. ``You know more about this than I do, and I'm the one in the chemistry club.''

``So what was that about bismuth being `inconvenient'?'' Watari pressed, sounding impatient---maybe because he didn't like Mikage praising me.

I let out a sigh before answering. ``Just because I used to be in the chemistry club, did you think I had the whole periodic table memorized? Mercury and gallium are one thing, but I'm not confident I remember where bismuth goes.''

``Then what are we supposed to do? We don't even know whether the other one is mercury or gallium.''

``I can't tell just by looking, but there is a way to tell them apart. They have different melting points. Mercury is almost always liquid at room temperature, but gallium melts at around 30\textdegree C, which means it'll solidify if we cool it down a little.''

``So how are we supposed to cool it? Leave it in this tunnel and wait around?''

``Leave it to me,'' Mikage said. ``I've got an idea.''

Mikage stepped out of the tunnel, went under the tarp, and spoke to the senior girl there.

``Um, I'm thirsty. Could we have something to drink?''

``Of course! For all three of you?''

``Just one is fine for now.''

The senior girl took a paper cup out of a bag and poured some tea from the jug. \textit{I see.}

Watari and I went back to the board too, and after the three of us exchanged a nod, we picked up Element Alpha. Without hesitation, we plunged the whole test tube into the tea.

The result was immediate. The surface of the liquid metal started to solidify. Since mercury needs a temperature of around minus 40\textdegree C to freeze, that meant the substance had to be gallium.

``Alright, Delta, where does gallium go?''

``I'm trying to remember that right now.''

I stepped back into the tunnel and faced the periodic table covering the wall. People usually memorize the top part with mnemonics like `H-He-Li-Be-B-C-N-O-F-Ne,' but for most people things start getting hazy around the twenty-first element, scandium (Sc). I was no exception. But there was a way to reason it out.

Gallium is a metal, but since it's also used as a semiconductor material, it should be near elemental semiconductors like silicon and germanium. It isn't a transition element, so it had to be somewhere from Group 13 onward. Germanium is in Group 14, same as silicon. That meant gallium was probably around Group 13. But was it in the fourth period or the fifth? I thought it was probably the fourth, but I couldn't be sure.

``You done yet, ex-chemistry boy?''

``Give me a second, physics boy.''

\textit{Got it. Point taken. My bad for being oversensitive there. I'll treat them as the generic suspects they are and move the story along.}

\textit{The reason I couldn't remember the periodic table clearly despite being in the chemistry club was definitely because I'd always relied on Mizasaki. That chemistry-obsessed geek, who could grin for hours just staring at the table, was always by my side during club activities. As a result, I'd completely offloaded all periodic-table knowledge onto him. Apparently, the hippocampus starts slacking off when there's always someone nearby to feed you the answers.}

\textit{Actually, wait.}

\textit{As Mizasaki's face came to mind, a bizarre phrase suddenly floated up in my memory.}

\textit{`---B-Al-Ga-In-Te-Li: Representing Japan.'}

\textit{It was a ridiculous mnemonic he'd shown off so proudly. It was completely absurd, making it sound as though a crowbar---or ``Bar-Al''---had some kind of advanced intelligence. But the meaning was clear enough. Boron and aluminum are both in Group 13. It was a phrase for memorizing Group 13.}

``It's below aluminum. The one-yen coin.''

``Took you long enough.''

Complaining as usual, Watari peeled the Ziploc bag off the grid space directly under the one-yen coin. I placed the gallium there as instructed.

What Watari pulled out was another square sheet of paper. Apparently, this was the investigative material.

\begin{quote}
Let me introduce the suspects' personal belongings. This information has been thoroughly confirmed.

\noindent $\blacksquare$ C-taro: A monkey wrench. It went missing on the morning of the incident and was later found near the scene.

\noindent $\blacksquare$ Doctor D: A key to a certain device. It went missing on the morning of the incident and was later found near the scene.

In addition, we have obtained photographs taken on the day of the incident. Please examine them.

\begin{flushright}
Great Detective Hongo Nagisa
\end{flushright}
\end{quote}

``What's Doctor D's `certain device'? The guy sounds suspicious just from the name.''

``I want to see the photos first,'' I said.

The senior handed us two photos. They were small, card-sized prints with wide white margins.

``These are Instax prints. Instant-camera photos,'' Mikage said.

The photos looked like snapshots of students at work. One showed a man and woman striking silly poses in front of the tall fir tree---the conical evergreen used as a Christmas tree---that served as the symbol of the Central Shopping District. The other showed the same two drinking iced coffee at a cafe in the same district. Maybe they were a couple? Narrow sticky notes had been attached next to each of their faces, labeling them B-ko and C-taro in flashy gal-style handwriting.

``Judging from the sky, both of these were taken around the same time---near sunset. Is this supposed to be an alibi?''

Mikage nodded at Watari's suddenly ominous observation.

``In the coffee photo, the sunset is reflecting off a nearby building. In the tree photo, the Christmas lights are on. If we can find out what time those lights come on, we can pin down when B-ko and C-taro were in the Central Shopping District, which might rule them out as suspects.''

Mikage was exactly right. Not wanting to get left behind, I jumped in too.

``So that means we'd better go to the Central Shopping District and check the lighting schedule?''

``Yeah. What a pain.''

Our list of things to do and places to visit had just grown by one more. \textit{Unease settled heavily in my chest.}

\textit{Could we really finish the first assignment within the time limit?}

``Look at this.''

Mikage pointed her long finger at the right side of the Instax prints.

``Both of them have three white dots on them. I think there's probably dirt on the rollers that push the film out. Maybe that's a clue to figuring out who took the photos.''

``Right. If the photographer was one of the suspects, not just B-ko and C-taro---''

I finished Watari's thought for him.

``Then we might be able to identify the culprit by confirming the alibis of three of the four suspects.''

``That's the idea. Assuming we're still only talking in hypotheses, of course. Unless there's testimony that the camera never left its owner's hands, we can't rule out the possibility that someone else took the photos.''

Watari checked his watch as though he'd suddenly remembered the time.

``It's 11:20. Mikage, do you think we can make the earlier bus?''

``There aren't many buses. We're probably better off taking a little time to rehydrate and catching the one at 11:45.''

We handed back the photos and asked someone to throw away the cup we'd used to cool the gallium test tube. In the slightly cool tunnel, I drank cold barley tea to quench my thirst while blankly staring at the periodic table covering the walls.

\textit{And we still have two more of these\dots}

$\gamma$

We made it back to the bus with plenty of time to spare and arrived safely at our second checkpoint, Tsunanagai Station. It was only 11:35. It looked like we'd still be able to take our lunch break even after finishing the station checkpoint. Unlike the buses heading toward Kannon-kutsu, the buses to the tunnel came often. As long as nothing major went wrong, we should be able to get back to school early.

``After seeing that, I feel a lot more at ease. Identifying the culprit looks like it'll be easier than I thought,'' Hijiri-kun said as we got off the bus and started looking for the checkpoint.

``I think Team X would be pretty disappointed if they ended up seeing that one last.''

``True. Especially Watari-kun---he was raised in England and apparently has strong opinions about mysteries, so it wouldn't be surprising if he got furious.''

\textit{I wasn't sure what being raised in England had to do with it. Granted, Doyle and Christie were British.}

``They're not here.''

Togakushi-kun said that while squinting against the glare. I realized that the organizers and camera crews who'd been waiting for us at Kannon-kutsu---even setting up a tarp tent---were nowhere to be seen in front of the station.

Hijiri-kun took out the map and showed it to us.

``Strictly speaking, the coordinates seem to point to the other side of the station. Since it's so busy here, this probably wasn't a very suitable place to set things up.''

``Let's go.''

As usual, Togakushi-kun headed toward the station without another word. He'd slept the whole way back on the bus, but he was still rubbing his eyes like he hadn't fully woken up yet.

Right next to Tsunanagai Station was a pedestrian bridge that let people and bicycles cross over the tracks to the seaside. As we crossed the bridge, I spotted a tarp tent set up along the tracks behind the station.

That was it.

We hurried toward it. I could see blond-haired Osawa-kun---the Physics Club guy who'd declared ``We're going to win'' that morning---sticking his head out of the tent and looking our way.

``Thanks for your hard work in this heat.''

When we reached the tarp tent, Hijiri-kun politely bowed. Bakuma-san---an alumnus and former member of the American football club---was sitting in a folding chair in the shade, chatting with some students.

Bakuma-san pulled at the front of his shirt, which was unbuttoned down to the second button, and laughed cheerfully.

``I should be the one saying that. I'm having a great time. You kids are amazing.''

And on the monitor he pointed to, there was a close-up of my face in profile. I was covered in sweat, which was a little embarrassing. At this checkpoint too, there was a separate camera crew waiting for us in addition to the two people following us around.

``Have you been here the whole time, Bakuma-san?''

Ignoring the small, impatient groan from Togakushi-kun, Hijiri-kun kept the conversation going.

``No. I've got a car, so I've been helping with odd jobs and driving supplies and students around. But yeah, I've been here for quite a while now.''

``I see. By the way, is Kurauchi-san---''

``I want to see the assignment.''

Togakushi-kun finally cut Hijiri-kun off and addressed the upperclassmen running the event.

``Oh, that's right. Here it is!''

The male student who had been talking with Bakuma-san showed us a board similar to the one at Kannon Cave.

\begin{quote}
There are two locked boxes on the table next to the tent. Choose one and open it.

The investigation materials that will serve as clues from me are stored inside.

Note: Two identical copies of the investigation materials have been prepared, printed on both sides, so please take only one.

\begin{flushright}
Great Detective☆Hongo Nagisa
\end{flushright}
\end{quote}

Before I even finished reading, Togakushi-kun had already moved to the side of the tent. There was a simple round table there, and on top of it sat two small boxes with lids shaped like treasure chests.

As I got closer, I saw that the boxes were secured with four-digit combination padlocks. What stood out was the strange object hanging from each padlock, something that looked like a piece of wire fencing.

``It's a resistor. It's been soldered on.''

Togakushi-kun picked up one of the wire meshes and brought it close to his face. I bent down a little to get a look too.

Thin wires had been attached to the body and shackle of the red padlock. Those wires were connected by carbon resistors arranged in a mesh pattern. It was like the lock itself had been built into a circuit.

A carbon resistor, incidentally, is a small, inexpensive component used to control electric current. Thin silver leads extended from the rice-grain-sized bodies, which were light brown with four colored bands. They had been soldered together in a lattice, making them look just like a wire fence.

``This is interesting. Using resistors to make a wire mesh. They probably wanted to install it on an actual fence by the tracks, but I guess they couldn't get permission from the railway company.''

There really was a wire fence along the tracks, and it even had gaps that looked like they could be neatly patched with a mesh woven out of resistors. \textit{It was interesting, but if I brought it up now, Togakushi-kun would probably just get annoyed.}

``We need a four-digit number to open this padlock. If we're talking numbers, it looks like it'd be the combined resistance of the mesh connected across the dial, but...''

``We need to calculate the equivalent resistance. I want to know the resistance of one resistor first.''

Togakushi-kun flattened the curled resistor mesh across his palm so we could see it better.

The carbon resistors formed a $3 \times 3$ diamond-shaped mesh, with twenty-four in total. Wires were connected diagonally to the diamond mesh from both the left and right. All the resistors were the same type. The color code was white, brown, brown, and gold. If we read that, we'd know the resistance of a single resistor.

``The colors represent numbers, right? You take the numbers from the first two colors as the tens and ones digits, then multiply that by ten to the power represented by the third color... was that how it worked?''

I had meant to check with Hijiri-kun, but for some reason he was distracted by a red sports car parked on the nearby street and had his back turned to me. Togakushi-kun clicked his tongue.

``Sorry, I'll work it out right now.''

I decided to try remembering it on my own. I remembered that the color codes more or less followed the colors of the rainbow. Zero was black, one was brown, and then it followed the rainbow from red to orange, yellow, green, and blue, skipping indigo and going to purple. Then gray. Then finally white.

``White is nine and brown is one, so ninety-one times ten to the first power makes the resistance nine hundred and ten... I think.''

I didn't say it with much confidence, and just then Hijiri-kun finally came back over to us.

``Sorry, I looked away for a second. Resistors... how nostalgic. We covered those in technology class back in middle school. That last gold band means it has a five percent tolerance. It doesn't really matter this time, though.''

Even while we were talking, Togakushi-kun's hands kept moving. He hadn't started calculating. He'd started turning the dial. He set the numbers to ``1690'' and pulled up on the shackle.

\textit{Click.} It actually opened.

``Wait, what kind of calculation was that?''

``I'll tell you while we're eating lunch.''

Togakushi-kun tossed the padlock, resistor mesh and all, onto the table and pulled a folded piece of paper out of the treasure chest. When he unfolded it, it turned into a square sheet. It was investigation material from Great Detective☆Hongo Nagisa.

\begin{quote}
Analysis of the footage recorded at various locations on the day of the incident is as follows.

\noindent $\blacksquare$ Fact 1: At the crime scene, the camera was running for a long duration. Removing the bolts from the scaffolding would inevitably have been caught on camera, but the power was cut from 6:55 PM until the incident occurred. Given testimony that someone crossed the scaffolding just before that, it can be stated with certainty that the bolts were removed during that timeframe.

\noindent $\blacksquare$ Fact 2: From 6:45 PM until the first report of the incident came in, Doctor D was in front of Tsunanagai Station being interviewed for a close-up segment about a new invention, and appeared on video the entire time.

In addition, important evidence discarded near the Central Shopping District has been obtained, so please check it.

\begin{flushright}
Great Detective Hongo Nagisa
\end{flushright}
\end{quote}

``In other words, even taking that into account, D is in the clear. If they were on camera in front of the station the whole time, there's no way they could've removed the bolts at Kannon Cave.''

``I think so too! Let's check the important evidence.''

Before I even had to say anything, Togakushi-kun had already gotten it from the upperclassman. It was a small clear zippered plastic bag. Inside was a long bolt, and written on the bag in marker were the words ``Bolt removed from the scaffolding at the time of the incident.'' The handwriting was rounded, neat, and kind of cute.

``What's with this bolt?''

Togakushi-kun muttered, looking exasperated. I borrowed it and peered at it too.

``...Ah! Hey, look at this!''

I cried out in excitement, louder than I'd meant to. The evidence was right there if you looked closely enough.

``I see. We haven't done the tunnel yet, but with this, we've basically identified the culprit.''

Hijiri-kun, who had been examining the bolt with his glasses pushed up onto his forehead, spoke with a satisfied air. Togakushi-kun nodded, handed the bolt back to the upperclassman, and then picked up the padlock he'd left on the table.

``I see the whole picture of the game now. There's one thing I want to test for later.''

I was a little surprised to hear a suggestion from Togakushi-kun, since he usually didn't seem like the type to take the lead.

And it was something neither Hijiri-kun nor I could have imagined.

$\delta$

Just as we got off the bus in front of the station at high noon, we ran into the three members of Team Y---whether that was good luck or bad, I couldn't say. Just like us, they seemed to be looking for somewhere to eat before rushing off to lunch.

In a situation like this, what were we supposed to do? Since both teams wanted to start lunch as soon as possible, any time spent talking was a loss. Still, it didn't feel right to just ignore them either. Iwama must have been thinking the same thing, because she was glancing our way hesitantly when our eyes met.

Expecting some dramatic encounter, both camera crews split into foreground and background positions and readied their equipment.

``Man, it's hot. Things going smoothly on your end?''

As expected, Hijiri was the one who cheerfully broke the ice. Beside him, white-haired Togakushi glared at Hijiri without even trying to hide how badly he wanted to start lunch already.

``I wouldn't call it smooth.''

When I answered, Hijiri came over looking oddly pleased.

``That's a shame. By the way, I wanted to apologize in advance. I did something I feel a little bad about.''

``What the hell is that supposed to mean?''

Hijiri suddenly threw a question at Watari, who had snapped that out sharply.

``Brown-black-red-gold---do you know how many ohms that is?''

``Why the hell should I have to tell you that?''

``It's one kilo-ohm, of course. Well, I'm glad you knew. That eases my guilt a little. Since we're both in a hurry, I'd hate to cut this short, but let's leave the greetings at that.''

As he said that, Hijiri motioned me over as if asking for a handshake. Then, when he actually took the hand I offered, he whispered to me.

``I'm also curious about the whereabouts of the Chemistry Club member.''

It was a single sentence, spoken in a seriousness completely unlike the cheerful tone he'd used until then.

``Got it.''

He must have chosen those words so only I would understand. We were wary of the guests---especially the two from outside the school. Bakuma-san was a former American football club member, and Kurauchi-san was a former Chemistry Club member. Hijiri must have run into Bakuma-san somewhere along the way. I took it to mean he wanted information on Kurauchi-san too.

Hijiri left to catch up with Togakushi, who had already started walking off.

Iwama was the last one still there. She gave the three of us a gentle smile and a small wave.

``Good luck.''

At the very end, it felt like her gaze lingered on me for just a second longer, but it was probably just my imagination. Nothing but self-consciousness. I couldn't think of a single thing to say back.

After that, we went into a Chinese restaurant in front of the station, and all three of us ordered large bowls of cold ramen. It wasn't the kind of place I'd normally go, but we picked it immediately because they accepted the meal vouchers and it was close to the bus stop. The vouchers were worth 1,000 yen each, and a large bowl of cold ramen cost exactly that. It came with chilled, chewy noodles in a broth flavored with soy sauce, vinegar, and sesame oil, topped with surprisingly luxurious ingredients like shrimp and jellyfish, and even included annin tofu for dessert. Maybe because we---mostly Mikage---were gulping down water so fast, the waiter left a pitcher of ice water on the table for us.

Since the rules required us to stay in the restaurant for thirty minutes, we used the time to rest our feet and figure out a plan. There was a timetable and route map at the information center for the buses leaving from the station. We spread it out on the table and started our strategy meeting.

That was when we ran into a troublesome fact.

Apparently, there weren't many buses going to Kannon Cave, and the next one wouldn't come until 1:20 PM. If we took that one, we'd arrive a little over thirty minutes later, but that would leave us with only a little over an hour to finish the task there and get back to school. There was no guarantee there'd be a return bus at the right time.

Watari checked the timetable against his G-SHOCK.

``Lunch break ends at 12:34. From there, we do the station task, check what time the tree lights up in the Central Shopping District, and catch the bus to Kannon Cave. The bus leaves the station at :20, so it should reach the shopping district around :25. We need to get all of that done in fifty minutes.''

Just thinking about it made my chest feel heavy. It would take nearly fifteen minutes just to walk to the shopping district.

``If we start running short on time, maybe we should jog between places?''

Mikage's suggestion was sounding more realistic by the second. In fact, our biggest concern was...

``We can't count on getting a bus back. We're seriously going to have to run from Kannon Cave to the school.''

Watari spread a map out over the timetable. Mikage used her finger to compare the scale bar against the distance.

``It's five kilometers as the crow flies. The actual route is probably just under seven. Even if we finish the task in fifteen minutes, we'd still have to head back at about a seven-minutes-per-kilometer pace and make it to the sub-control room to name the culprit.''

``It's all downhill. And the road shouldn't be crowded. Five minutes per kilometer should be easy.''

``Are you serious?''

``What, worried about your stamina, former Chemistry Club member?''

``I didn't say I couldn't run. I just said I'd rather not.''

I was about to say this was turning into orienteering before remembering that it technically already was orienteering. \textit{Were we really going to run? In this scorching heat? Besides, what about the mystery? Did he expect me to think while running at a five-minute pace?}

From the next table over, the two-person camera crew was filming us. They'd already gotten permission from the staff to film us earlier. \textit{Seeing that two athletic-looking people had been assigned to Team X's crew, I started to think Hongo-senpai had predicted all too accurately that we'd end up like this.}

Beep, beep, beep, beep. The \textsc{g-shock} went off.

Five minutes before we had to leave the restaurant.

Watari flashed a fearless grin.

``Alright, everyone use the restroom? We might only be a third of the way through, but from here on out, it's a dead sprint to the finish.''

$\gamma$

``I wonder if that method was really okay.''

As I watched the three of them go into the Chinese restaurant and voiced my concern, Togakushi-kun spoke up.

``That move was to test exactly that. They didn't say anything, which means it's fine.''

``I think it was a very good idea. The rules only said, `No interference that would make continuation impossible.' Which means that as long as the other side can still continue, it's fair game. In a first assignment that isn't a time attack, testing that now is actually the fairer move.''

They'd probably realize our sabotage soon enough. I prayed they wouldn't take it as simple harassment, but as a message: \textit{We've confirmed that this is how the rules work.}

``This is the place. We can use the vouchers here.''

The place Togakushi-kun chose was---surprisingly, though maybe that was rude of me to think---a fairly stylish cafe. Since we could only spend up to 1,000 yen each, we looked for lunch menu items that fit the limit.

``Sorry, but I have a favor to ask you two.''

The moment we opened the menus, Togakushi-kun spoke up.

``You can choose a drink with your meal, right? Could you both make yours iced coffee? I'll pay you back 1,000 yen each once the Science Orient is over. I want you to give me your iced coffees.''

Togakushi-kun glanced over at the two camera crew members. They were in the middle of negotiating filming permission with the waiter.

``You're probably wondering why, so I'll tell only you two. At this rate, there's a chance I'm going to run out of battery. I want to get as much caffeine into my system as I can while I still have the chance.''

``By `run out of battery,' do you mean you'll get sleepy?''

``Something like that. It's not just that I get sleepy. I'll actually fall asleep. I don't want to be a burden. So please.''

Togakushi-kun bowed his head to us. \textit{I panicked.}

``I don't mind at all! But I don't think that's good for your health. Caffeine's a diuretic too.''

``It's fine. I usually rely on caffeine pills and energy drinks. But those were banned from the Science Orient. I was able to get some headache medicine from the camera crew, though, since therapeutic drugs were allowed.''

With that, Togakushi-kun took two tablets from his breast pocket and swallowed them with water. They were aspirin.

``Do you really need three cups?''

Hijiri-kun asked, sounding perfectly composed. \textit{Three cups really did seem like too much.}

``Once we're moving, I won't be able to drink anything, so I'm loading up now. Don't worry. If there isn't a restroom, I'll just find somewhere to take care of it.''

\textit{That was just too pitiful.} I tried to think of a better way.

``...Hey, how about this?''

Togakushi-kun seemed to like the idea that suddenly came to me.

When we placed our order, I asked for my iced coffee in a takeout cup.

$\delta$

What Hijiri had been apologizing for became obvious the moment we reached the checkpoint behind the station.

Two treasure chests sat on the table. One had already been opened by Team Y and was empty.

The other had two combination padlocks on it---one red and one blue.

According to Watari, the task involved calculating something called equivalent resistance, but the two locks used different kinds of resistors and were wired in different configurations. We were already short on time, and now Team Y's sabotage was forcing us to do twice the calculations.

``I'll kill him... that shitty humanities-glasses brat!''

Watari cursed under his breath as he sketched the resistor mesh onto a sheet of copy paper.

``Using `shitty' isn't very nice.''

``Shut up, you shitty science-glasses brat.''

Mikage, peering over Watari's hand, cut in without batting an eye at our exchange.

``For the second one, if you multiply 910 by thirteen-sevenths, it comes out to 1690.''

``I'll open it.''

While Watari was still calculating, I tried setting the red padlock---which looked like it had been added afterward---to ``1690.'' \textit{Click.} It opened. Then Watari opened the other one, and a square sheet of investigation material came out from inside.

It was a record about video evidence from the day of the incident. The time of the crime was between 6:55 \textsc{pm} and 7:05 \textsc{pm}. And D had an alibi for that time.

``The time of the crime is right around sunset. The tree lighting is probably at 7:00 or 7:30. Put that together with the cafe photo from before sunset, and B and C's alibis are established.''

Watari was already theorizing about the illumination, even though we hadn't checked it yet. \textit{Working backward from the conditions for an alibi to predict what the evidence would say was an incredibly meta way to solve it. He could probably think that way so easily because he was used to dirty tricks like guessing the content of classical Japanese passages from the multiple-choice answers.}

``So if B, C, and D all have alibis, does that mean the culprit is the one left over, A?''

Watari tilted his head at Mikage's deduction.

``If you look at it simply, yeah. But there's no way that malicious, shitty woman would make the plot that straightforward. If it all came down to an alibi this simple, we wouldn't need information about the other belongings.''

``Should we take a look at this `important evidence'?''

When I suggested it, the upperclassman handed over the bolt in the bag. Watari examined it immediately.

``The bolt removed from the scaffolding, huh? It's the kind you could easily loosen with the monkey wrench C lost. The culprit might've stolen the wrench from C to make it look like he did it.''

``Something's caught in it.''

Mikage pointed at the threaded part of the bolt. The three of us leaned in for a closer look.

Now that she pointed it out, there really were fibers caught in the threads. They were as fine as dust, but some were red and some were transparent. They were probably the same kind of fiber.

``There's some lint caught here. Can you tell what kind of material it is, former Chemistry Club member? Natural, plant-based, or synthetic?''

``There's no way I can tell just from this. I might be able to narrow it down a little if I'm allowed to singe it.''

``In the photos, neither B nor C was wearing red. Though we can't check their underwear.''

``As if any high schooler would be wearing red underwear like that.''

Mikage merely gave the overly certain Watari a meaningful smile without saying a word. \textit{Don't go silent there.}

Watari cleared his throat and looked at the \textsc{g-shock} on his right wrist with a somewhat exaggerated motion.

``We're out of time. We'll think while we head for the Central Shopping District.''

$\gamma$

``The tunnel task was over in an instant. Hijiri-kun had the periodic table memorized.''

``Let's go with bismuth. Atomic number 83, period 6, group 15.''

He said it without the slightest hesitation and picked out the iridescent, shimmering crystal. His memory was almost frightening.

``You know everything, don't you, Hijiri-kun?''

``I don't know everything. Just the things I've learned.''

He must have been dying to say that line. Hijiri-kun puffed out his chest, looking immensely satisfied.

$\delta$

After checking the Central Shopping District, we found a sign stating that the tree illumination would be on from exactly 7:00 \textsc{pm} until exactly 10:00 \textsc{pm}. For some reason, the sign looked brand new, as if it had only just been installed. It might have been put up specifically for the Science Orient to help establish an alibi.

As for the sunset, none of us remembered the exact time, but we all agreed it had been a little before 7:00 \textsc{pm}. We confirmed that the two Instax photos of B-ko and C-taro had been taken close together; the cafe photo with the setting sun hitting the building was taken before 7:00 \textsc{pm}, while the photo with the illumination lit was taken after 7:00 \textsc{pm}.

In other words, around 7:00 \textsc{pm}---the time of the crime---B and C were here near the Central Shopping District.

``The crime scene is probably Kannon Cave, which we still haven't been to. Even by taxi, a round trip would take twenty minutes. There'd be no way for B and C to have committed the crime. D is ruled out too, so if it's really just a four-choice question, then A has to be the culprit.''

``Are we still going to Kannon Cave anyway?''

Since it was obvious we'd have to run on the way back, I really wanted to avoid it. But Watari nodded at me.

``Of course we are. We were told it's better to visit all the checkpoints even if we already know who the culprit is. If they went out of their way to say that, it means we have to go if we want to win.''

Mikage also seemed completely ready to run, so I quietly gave in and boarded the bus bound for Okutsunanagai.

1:25 \textsc{pm}.

Only one hour and thirty-five minutes remained before the time limit.

$\gamma$

By the time we finished all the checkpoints and got back to Tsunanagai Station, it was 1:30 \textsc{pm}.

Even though we could've made it in time on foot, we decided to save our strength and got on a bus that passed in front of the school.

We knew who the culprit was. There were no mysteries left in the case. Things had gone so smoothly that it was starting to make me uneasy. As the bus swayed along, I went back over everything in my head, checking for anything we might have missed.

``Would it be alright if I'm the one who names the culprit to Hongo-senpai?''

Since Togakushi-kun had his eyes closed, Hijiri-kun was basically asking me.

``Of course! Actually, could we ask you to do it?''

``Yeah. I'd be happy to do it. I've always wanted to do that whole `The culprit is so-and-so' thing.''

$\delta$

As soon as the bus reached the Kannon Cave stop and we confirmed at the stop across the road that there were no buses heading back, we ran toward the tarp we could see in the distance. As we crossed the suspension bridge with the camera filming us head-on, the event staff stood up as if they'd been waiting for us. Mizasaki waved at us. Beside him was the guest, Kurauchi-san. Hijiri had said he was curious about Kurauchi-san's movements, but we didn't have the luxury of investigating him. I figured I could just ask Mizasaki later. We took turns spraying on the provided insect repellent while reading the assignment board. It explained where the clue had been hidden.

Apparently, we had to choose either three Arakashi trees or four Shirakashi trees and find the centroid of their roots. But Team Y had already dug up the Arakashi side. Only the Shirakashi option remained.

``Shirakashi has long, narrow leaves with serrations along the upper two-thirds. Be careful, because the ones with white undersides belong to a different tree called Urajirogashi.''

``What the hell do you mean by `serrations'? Speak Japanese.''

``Fine, I'll find the first one, so just look at these leaves.''

While I searched for a Shirakashi, Mikage was staring down at the ground, looking troubled. Once I'd finally found the first one and plucked a leaf from a low branch, Mikage spoke to me.

``When they say `centroid,' do they mean the actual centroid of a quadrilateral? Or the average of the coordinates of the vertices?''

``...What do you mean by that?''

Beside me, while I was still standing there holding the leaf like an idiot, Watari clicked his tongue.

``I see. For a triangle, they're the same, but for a quadrilateral, they aren't necessarily identical.''

``How about we think about that while we look for the four Shirakashi? We don't have time.''

I didn't really understand the part about the centroid, so I urged them to keep moving. Watari and Mikage each took a Shirakashi leaf for reference, and the three of us split up to look for the remaining three.

Since there were Arakashi and even Urajirogashi growing there too, which made things incredibly confusing, I ended up finding two more myself. One left. But I couldn't find it.

``Fifty-five minutes until the time limit. This is getting bad.''

Watari snapped irritably. We still had time, but every moment we wasted here would make the run back that much harsher. We still had to run nearly seven kilometers.

``A tetrahedron.''

Mikage suddenly looked like she'd had a flash of inspiration. I listened.

``It might not all be on a flat plane; the four points could be arranged three-dimensionally. In a tetrahedron, the actual centroid and the average of the coordinates are the same, so the term `centroid' defines it uniquely.''

In other words, she was saying we needed to think in three dimensions. I looked around. Our search area was a fifty-meter square east of the tent. At its northern edge was a rocky cliff face. Up there---

``There! That's it!''

Even from a distance, I could tell from the overall shape and impression. A single Shirakashi tree was growing on top of the cliff.

``How do we find the centroid of a tetrahedron?''

``It's on the line segment connecting a vertex to the centroid of the opposite triangle. It's at the point three-quarters of the way down from the vertex.''

``Watari! Close enough is fine! Just tell me the centroid of the first three we found!''

``Thirty seconds!''

Watari ran from one Shirakashi to the next. Mikage followed his movements and helped with the calculation.

``Watari-kun, stand at the midpoint of those two. I'll stand at the remaining one.''

``Got it. Delta! The centroid is the point that divides the line between Mikage and me in a two-to-one ratio!''

I finally understood what the two of them were doing, and I could see the triangle on the ground in my mind. The point that divides the line from a vertex to the midpoint of the opposite side in a two-to-one ratio is the centroid of a triangle. And the centroid of the tetrahedron lay somewhere on the line segment from the vertex atop the cliff to the centroid of the triangle on the ground.

The shape of a tetrahedron, formed by four triangles, took shape in the forest. Partway along the line dropping from the vertex to the triangle's centroid stood the trunk of a Konara oak---

When I rushed over, I found one unnatural spot on the surface of the trunk. The bark of a Konara, which normally has distinctive deep vertical grooves, was smooth and flat in that one patch. Looking closer, I saw a piece of gray vinyl, colored to match the trunk, pasted there. But it was out of my reach.

``Delta-kun, get on.''

As I stood there staring blankly upward, Mikage came up behind me and, without a moment's hesitation, ducked her head between my slightly parted legs. \textit{It genuinely startled me.}

``Get on what?''

``On my shoulders.''

It felt like she'd just hit me with a wrestling move. As I jerked my hips back so I wouldn't smack the back of Mikage's head, her shoulders and arms locked my thighs firmly in place. My body lifted into the air. Feeling like I might fall, I leaned forward, and Mikage's ponytail brushed against my lower abdomen. Her hair softened the impact enough that I avoided hitting her directly. The piece of vinyl was right in front of my face.

Before I could even process what was happening to me, I peeled it off.

``I'm wearing a skirt, so I figured it made more sense for me to be on the bottom,'' Mikage said calmly after crouching down to let me off.

\textit{Did I just get forced into a shoulder carry by a girl in my class? Was something like that even allowed? Or rather, was something like that even possible with a girl's physical strength?}

Even Watari couldn't hide his surprise, but he came over and pushed me on.

``Come on. The last clue.''

I hurriedly opened the Ziploc bag stuck to the back of the vinyl. The three of us read it together.

``...Wait, something's off here,'' Watari said first. He was right. Something was off. The person who had taken the Instax photos that established B and C's alibis was A. The white spots on the photos we'd been shown at the tunnel matched the details written in the investigation materials here exactly. And since the report said A never let the camera out of his sight, there was no way someone else had taken them instead. Which meant A had an alibi too.

None of the four suspects could have come anywhere near Kannon Cave---the crime scene---at the time it happened.

``I wonder if the fibers caught on the bolt---the important evidence---came from B's charm.''

I nodded at Mikage's observation.

``So after removing the bolt with the stolen monkey wrench, they hid it inside the charm and kept it on them... though B has an alibi, obviously.''

``Fine. Let's go check the crime scene and the last piece of evidence.''

Watari immediately switched gears and started moving. Mikage and I followed after him.

Kannon Cave sat partway up a cliff overlooking the river. A narrow path carved into the cliff led to the cave, and from there a stretch of scaffolding---the supposed murder scene---had been set up. It definitely looked like a fall from there would be fatal. Far below, a river flowed past huge boulders. Life-threatening---

``Wait.''

Partway down the path to Kannon Cave, I stopped Watari.

``What is it?''

``I'll go first. There's something I want to check.''

Without waiting for an answer, I pushed past Watari. The path was narrow, so I ended up pressing him against the rock wall, but there was no helping it. If anything was going to happen, I felt like it would be here.

\textit{...Cause an injury. Cause a death. Burn down a building. Destroy something precious. If I make it look like an irreversible accident happened because of the Biology Club, I can even deal them a massive blow.}

Until now, there hadn't really been any point where an accident felt likely. But here. If a bolt really had been removed, just like in the ``Science Orient Murder Case.'' \textit{Maybe I was overthinking it, but I couldn't dismiss the possibility, however remote. Hijiri and I had received what looked like either a murder threat or a warning.}

The trees above the river were sparse, and the midday sun beat down from overhead. Sweat poured off me, but it probably wasn't just because of the heat.

At the front, with both hands braced against the rock, I cautiously stepped onto the scaffolding.

\textit{Creak.} It groaned, but it didn't give way. When I put more of my weight on it---

My body lurched into the air.

I thought my heart was going to stop. With a heavy, dull thud, the joint in the pipe supporting the planks gave way. The metal plank I'd been standing on crashed into the surrounding steel frame with a deafening clang before dropping toward the river below.

I'd been distracted. Careless. I felt myself being yanked backward. Watari had grabbed my arm and Mikage my waist, both moving with incredible speed. \textit{I had almost fallen.}

``Hey, are you okay!?'' the second-year camera assistant shouted from behind us. I was too stunned to move.

``Keep filming the fallen scaffold! I'll go call the people at the tent!''

With that, he turned and ran back. The cameraman aimed his lens at the scaffolding that had fallen to the bottom of the ravine.

With Watari and Mikage pulling me, I finally got my balance back onto the path in front of Kannon Cave. My breathing was ragged. \textit{I couldn't believe it. I'd been praying it was all in my head.}

``What the hell is this? Is she screwing with us?''

Watari cursed in a tone that was almost unnervingly calm. It sounded like he thought this was one of Hongo-senpai's sick jokes. \textit{I still couldn't completely rule that out...}

Behind Mikage, the cameraman was talking through his earpiece.

``The scaffolding fell! You can see it, right!?''

Some kind of response came through, and the cameraman pressed a hand to his earpiece and listened for a moment. Then he told us something unbelievable.

``Hongo-senpai says to continue. If you're trying to clear the assignment, keep moving forward.''

\textit{Keep moving forward, but how...?} Just as I was thinking that, Watari grabbed the frame of the scaffold and looked it over suspiciously. Metal pipes ran overhead at just the right height to grab if you reached up.

``This part looks fine. We can get across by hanging onto these and stepping on the steel frame.''

``But... the scaffolding fell.''

``You have eyes, don't you? This part of the frame is fine. Only the pipe supporting the boards came loose. That doesn't have much to do with the overall strength. We're running out of time. You really want to run seven kilometers flat out?''

Without waiting for my answer, Watari skillfully worked his way across the frame. Mikage put a hand on my shoulder.

``I'll make sure you're safe, Delta-kun. Let's cross quickly.''

\textit{The words normalcy bias flashed through my mind. It's that bad human habit of assuming you'll be fine even when you're staring danger in the face.}

``Let's be really careful.''

Watari had already made it across. The gap was short enough that you could probably jump it with a running start, though not on a path this narrow. I crossed the frame with Mikage holding onto my belt, and Watari grabbed my hand on the other side and pulled me over. When Mikage crossed next, I braced myself against the rock and reached out to her. Mikage gripped my hand firmly. I pulled her across, and the three of us made it over.

``What a sick joke. I'm protesting the second we get back.''

Watari stomped further down the path, visibly irritated. After passing a steep climb that led up toward the top of Kannon Cave, we reached an open space. In the middle of the trees sat something completely out of place.

It was, by all appearances, a hula hoop. But it came with an explanation that made us doubt what we were seeing.

We stood there speechless for a while before finally becoming certain who the culprit was.

$\gamma$

``The culprit is B-ko.''

In the sub-control room, Hijiri-kun declared it to Hongo-senpai.

``B-ko stole the key from Doctor D and used `the device' to teleport from the Central Shopping District to the back of Kannon Cave. After removing the bolt with the monkey wrench stolen from C-taro and hiding it inside the red-and-white charm, she used `the device' to return to the shopping district. The evidence is the red and white fibers caught in the bolt.''

``Corre---ct! That's a perfect deduction!''

Senpai applauded with an innocent smile.

$\delta$

\begin{center}
Doctor D's new invention: The Ring-Type High-Dimensional Transfer Device.

With this, anyone can travel instantly no matter the distance. Revolutionary!
\end{center}

The caption was written in Hongo-senpai's gyaru-style handwriting.

``The idiot who came up with a garbage trick like this doesn't deserve heaven or hell, you shitty bitch!''

Watari spat out the curse. \textit{I felt exactly the same way. Far from revolutionary, the whole setting had suddenly turned into straight-up sci-fi.}

``\dots Let's just get home.''

After that, it was hell. Between the scaffolding incident and everything else, we only had thirty-eight minutes left. Worried that a five-minute-per-kilometer pace wouldn't be enough, Watari ran out front at an even faster pace, while Mikage and I desperately chased after him. Mikage still looked perfectly composed, but I thought I was going to die. \textit{I shouldn't have ordered the large serving of cold ramen.}

We made it to the sub-control room with no time to spare. Watari simply said, ``B,'' and the first assignment came to an end.

There were cryptic words written on the backs of the investigation materials we'd gathered, and they seemed like they'd matter for the later assignments---but I was in no mood to think about that now.

\textit{My worst fears had come true. An accident had happened that could easily have gotten someone hurt.}

\textit{And it had happened in a situation that seemed to mirror the supposedly fictional ``Science Orient Murder Case''---}

% ==============================
%        Fragment
% ==============================
\clearpage
\thispagestyle{plain}
\phantomsection
\begin{center}
    \large\bfseries
    Fragment\\
    Conviction of a Specialist
\end{center}
\label{sec:fragConviction}
\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Fragment}
\vspace{1cm}

I didn't have many memories from early childhood, but for some reason, I vividly remembered my father seeing me eat nothing but cherry tomatoes and saying, ``Sho, you're a specialist.'' It was a biology joke.

To a biologist, a ``specialist'' didn't mean a craftsman or a professional. Cabbage White larvae ate only the leaves of the Brassicaceae family, Common Yellow Swallowtail larvae ate only the leaves of the Rutaceae family, and some species of fig wasps even shared a one-to-one relationship with specific types of figs---an ``obligate pollination mutualism'' in which, if one went extinct, the other would follow. Organisms that lived such specialized lives, focusing on specific foods or environments, were called specialists.

In other words, it meant being a picky eater. The larva known as Sho Izuta survived on cherry tomatoes. \textit{Apparently, my picky eating started to improve around the time my younger sister turned into a talkative little brat.}

Just as there were countless specialist species in the world, being one wasn't necessarily a bad thing. Plants often had defensive substances in their leaves to keep from being eaten, but Cabbage White larvae, for instance, could detoxify glucosinolates---substances unique to the Brassicaceae family---inside their bodies. It was something ``generalists,'' which ate all kinds of things, couldn't replicate. If an organism tried to produce all kinds of enzymes to break down every possible toxin, it would interfere with its other life processes. That was why creatures that ate many different kinds of leaves had to forage cleverly, folding a leaf and eating only the center, for example, where the toxins were less concentrated.

Specialists had their own survival strategies. It was that simple.

But what happened when I applied this idea of a ``specialist'' to humans in modern society? Was a way of life that specialized in a specific environment, not just in food, really desirable?

\textit{I didn't think so.}

\textit{Just as someone who ate nothing but cherry tomatoes would obviously struggle in social life, setting aside the nutritional issues, being a specialist was a pretty difficult way to live. You might wonder how I knew that. The answer was that I had actually lived that way.}

\textit{``Shade-dwellers'' and ``specialists'' were concepts that naturally fit well together. In modern society, a lifestyle of throwing everything else away to immerse yourself in one thing wasn't exactly recommended, unless you were an athlete or an artist. If you threw away the social skills and adaptability expected of a functioning member of society, people naturally started looking at you coldly. The more you immersed yourself in something, the more you drifted away from the mainstream of society. It was precisely the shade-dwellers---people who could quietly live out of sight without worrying about that sort of thing---who were suited to being specialists.}

\textit{That was how I had lived. Up through elementary school, under the influence of my father, a plant ecology researcher, I had immersed myself in plants, and from middle school onward, I had immersed myself in studying to beat my rival, Watari. I poured my energy only into the things I wanted to do at the time. I felt that even the time spent worrying about other things---sports, relationships, all of it---was a waste.}

\textit{If a generalist was versatile and well-rounded, then a specialist was someone who narrowed their focus. It was a way of life built on dodging all sorts of social pressures so you could cling to the things you wanted to do or were good at. A life spent clinging to your passions, whether people called you an otaku or a shade-dweller.}

\textit{Put like that, it might even sound like a good thing.}

\textit{But in reality, it was a huge mistake.}

\textit{Once, while we were looking at a great camphor tree that was twelve hundred years old, my father told me, ``You're going to become this someday.'' He told me it was fine if no one paid any attention to me out in the mountains. It was fine to be called a shade-dweller. He told me to live with the goal of becoming a giant tree that no one could ever complain about someday. My name, ``Sho,'' meant ``camphor tree,'' after all. But my father had left out one important point.}

\textit{Not every camphor tree became a giant.}

\textit{Only a tiny handful ever lived for hundreds of years, let alone a thousand. They were the lucky few that survived. All the others withered away in obscurity. Without anyone noticing them. Without even getting enough light.}

\textit{There must have been countless people whose hearts broke along the way, who gave up on living for what they loved.}

\textit{I was one of them. When I was little, I had innocently dreamed of becoming a botanist like my father, but not anymore. There was simply no way I could compete with that abnormal man, who had lived like a rootless weed, sacrificing every human relationship and every shred of social life to bury himself in research until my mother picked him up and they accidentally ended up having a child. Whether I liked it or not, I had realized that.}

\textit{Only those who survived fierce competition could spend their lives doing nothing but what they loved.}

\textit{So I ran away, and that was when I found studying. Schoolwork, where your results were clearly reflected in test scores, was simple and fun. While fooling around with Mizasaki in the Chemistry Club, competing with Watari from the rival Physics Club over test scores had somehow become a normal part of my life.}

\textit{My father had looked at me with disappointment and said, ``You're starting to take after your mother.'' Unlike my father, who had practically dropped out of society to bury himself in plants and trees, my mother had apparently been a top student who graduated first in her department.}

\textit{To my parents, it was probably just an old story, but hearing it actually scared me. What if I was just some half-baked specialist who had only inherited a little from both my father and my mother? What if I was just an ordinary person who, while trying to specialize in something, had simply given up on everything else?}

\textit{For a specialist built on focus and specialization, the most terrifying thing was probably losing in the very field they thought they were specialized in. And if they lost to a generalist who could handle everything else with ease, they would have nowhere left to stand.}

And then I encountered them.

The cherry blossoms blooming in the dazzling sunlight. The overwhelming light itself.

% ==============================
%        Chapter 3
% ==============================
\clearpage
\thispagestyle{plain}
\phantomsection
\begin{center}
    \large\bfseries
    Chapter 3\\
    The Science Game
\end{center}
\label{sec:ch3V3}
\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Chapter 3}
\vspace{1cm}

$\delta$

``Team Y was absolutely insane---they were basically cheating! They cleared it with near-theoretical perfection.''

Mizasaki spoke to me with a note of excitement as I sprawled out on the freezing floor of the air-conditioned meeting room, trying to soak up every last bit of cool air. The room looked empty except for the two of us. Or rather, it seemed Mizasaki had shown up to wake me from my nap. I had no idea what the rest of the situation was. For some reason, my glasses were gone, and everything was a blur.

``By the way, what time is it?''

``Disposable syringe.''

``I'm not in the mood for that right now.''

``It's just past 15:30. I just got back myself, but where are the other members?''

``Who knows. If they aren't here, I don't know where they are.''

``Don't give me that `bored convenience store clerk' routine. Why is Delta the only one passed out in the waiting room?''

``Because I got forced to run nearly seven kilometers under the blazing sun and staggered back to school half-dead.''

``That final push was incredible. Team X wasn't bad either. Team Y stole the spotlight, but they still put on a good showing. They fought with muscle, not just brainpower.''

\textit{It wasn't that we chose to rely on it so much as that we didn't have a choice.}

``How about Team Z? Did they manage to solve it?''

``Of course. We were watching the footage, so the second Team Y found the last clue, we figured it out too. Toudo, who was with the school standby group, turned in the answer for us. Every team cleared the first objective.''

I was just about to say that was a relief when it finally hit me and I fully woke up. Maybe because the heat and exhaustion had finally worn off, a heavy, icy reality suddenly came flooding through my veins. I finally pushed myself off the floor, dragged my sluggish body upright, and dropped into a nearby chair. I found my glasses, which I must have put on the desk at some point, and put them on.

``\dots\dots What happened with the accident?''

``Accident?''

Mizasaki sounded so blatantly fake that, for a second, I almost thought the whole thing had been a bad dream. But that wasn't the case. I could still remember it vividly.

``The scaffolding fell. Over at Kannon-kutsu.''

``Oh, that. We already took care of it. Bakuma-san rushed over, pulled on some boots, and hauled it out of the river. No problem.''

``No problem? Someone almost died!''

``Yeah, it was definitely a close call. I'm just glad Delta didn't get hurt.''

I was starting to get irritated by how utterly unconcerned he sounded, but then again, Mizasaki hadn't seen that card that looked practically like a murder threat. Since the game had gone on like nothing happened, maybe Mizasaki's reaction was the normal one. The scaffolding had simply fallen. Unlike Hongo-senpai's fiction, no one had actually died.

``Well, it was a pretty creepy coincidence, considering the whole setup of the `Science Department Orientation Murder Case'.''

``\dots\dots Isn't it possible it wasn't a coincidence? Like someone pulled the bolts to copy it?''

Mizasaki answered my grim suspicion in an oddly light tone.

``Hmm, I wonder if anyone would've even had the chance. For starters, there were no problems when Team Y crossed it this morning. I was around that area the whole time after that, and I don't think anyone from the management team went anywhere near the scaffolding unless they were filming.''

``You weren't watching it every second, were you? If someone planned it right, they could've found an opening.''

``I'm not so sure. To begin with, that whole murder mystery story was a complete surprise even to the management team. Until Delta and the others got the challenge letter detailing the incident at 10:00, no one knew the murder was supposed to have taken place on the Kannon-kutsu scaffolding. Aside from the three club presidents, of course.''

\textit{So that was how it worked?} It was true that when Hongo-senpai's handouts were shown on camera, the upperclassmen watching in the audiovisual room had started whispering. \textit{This mystery-style game must have been put together so only the people at the top knew the full picture. It felt like some absurdly classified state operation.}

That left three possibilities.

\begin{enumerate}
\item The scaffolding collapse was a tasteless stunt set up by Hongo-senpai.
\item Someone deliberately plotted a crime based on Hongo-senpai's game after 10:00.
\item The coincidence between the game and the accident was just that---a coincidence.
\end{enumerate}

That was the breakdown of possibilities: if I traced the cause of the scaffolding collapse as though I were following the game's plot, should I pin it on its creator, Hongo-senpai; on someone influenced by the game; or on something else entirely? I couldn't rule out the third option, but it didn't feel likely. I'd know whether Hongo-senpai was behind it if I asked her directly.

The one I needed to consider right now was the second theory: that someone had plotted a crime based on the game.

``Mizasaki, wouldn't there be footage if it happened after 10:00? Like a camera locked on the scaffolding in a long take?''

``Footage... No, that was just a plot point in the game to narrow down the time of the crime. There's no point filming scaffolding when nobody's there. The only cameras should've been the ones following Team X and Team Y, plus the ones stationed at each checkpoint. They were all handheld by cameramen.''

Just as I was thinking that made sense, Mizasaki's expression shifted like he'd suddenly remembered something.

``Oh, crap. I wasn't supposed to say that.''

``What weren't you supposed to tell me?''

``Sorry, sorry. I didn't mean to let anything slip. I'm on Team Z too, remember, and since this is a competition, it'd be bad if I told Delta anything that gave him an edge... You know, because yakiniku's on the line.''

The way he said that was a little suspicious, but it didn't seem directly related to the incident.

``Well, if you don't mind, could you tell me about the guests who were there at Kannon-kutsu? Kurauchi-san was there when we arrived, right?''

``Wait, are you suspecting Kurauchi-san? He's a really good guy.''
